==PAGE:4==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
3 
Abstract 
This thesis has been written as a part of phase 2 of the ongoing research project ‘Ventilation Conditions in 
Eaves and Upper Attics in Structures with Vapor-Open Roofing Underlays’, at the at the Department of Civil 
Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark, Kgs. Lyngby, Denmark. The aim of this research project 
is to test the Danish recommendation regarding design of cold attic rooms in single-family houses, by an 
experimental setup at the test grounds of the Department of Civil Engineering. This setup have been de-
signed to test parameter variations in north turned cold attic rooms, with a width of one meter and a leak-
age in the vapor barrier within the allowed according to Danish Building Regulations.  
The general recommendation is that cold attic rooms with a diffusion open roofing underlay should at least 
have some ventilation valves installed in order to ensure a ‘modest ventilation’. However, the recently fin-
ished phase 1 of the project indicated that having openings in the underlay for ventilation could cause more 
moisture problems than not having ventilation openings, even though construction standards had been 
followed. Phase 2 is made as a more extensive experimental setup, where more design variations are inves-
tigated and the influence of having a diffusion tight underlay is also investigated. 
In this thesis, measurement data from the experimental setup is analyzed for finding out, how different 
variations in the ventilation and underlay design influences the moisture conditions, depending on leakages 
in the vapor barrier in the building envelope. Besides measurements in the experimental setup, a numerical 
model is also developed using Computational Fluid Dynamics. This can be used for estimating what influ-
ence parameter changes has on the flow rates and patterns inside a cold attic room. Furthermore, a theo-
retical model of air flows is also developed for finding out if this supports the flows measured and simulat-
ed. 
Results from this project indicate that a diffusion open roofing underlay in general causes fewer problems 
with moisture, than if a diffusion tight underlay is used. Underlay valves should in general not be used in an 
attic room with a diffusion open underlay, since this potentially can increase infiltration to the attic room, if 
there is a leakage in the vapor barrier. Especially having only an opening in the top of the attic room creates 
a dangerous situation, since negative pressure under the roofing plates can propagate to the attic room 
and increase infiltration rates. If a diffusion tight underlay is used, even though this is not recommendable, 
the cold attic room should be ventilated with openings in the underlay near eaves and the top of the attic, 
in order to remove incoming moisture.

Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
8 
1. Introduction 
1.1. Motivation 
Moisture in roof constructions can cause problems, in form of degradation and mold growth, if the humidi-
ty is high for too long. Mold in constructions can result in health problems if occupants are exposed to air-
borne spores, mycotoxins or other products of mold. When exposed for some period, these products might 
irritate, and exacerbate allergic and asthmatic symptoms, irritate eyes, nose and throat, and increase the 
risk of respiratory infections. Long-term exposure to mycotoxins could cause an irritation of the immune 
system leading to inflammation. (Ryan KJ; Ray CG, ed., 2004) Exposure to the mentioned products could 
happen if, for example heated roof area is used for occupancy or if air from the roof enters the dwelling 
when the pressure conditions allows this. Moisture-related problems in a roof construction might also 
cause economic issues, since knowledge about the presence of mold in a building, and the potential nega-
tive health effect, lowers the value of the estate. 
Three different sources can be identified to contribute to the moisture in a cold attic rooms. Moist indoor 
air infiltrating up to the roof construction and in to the attic room if there are leakages in the vapor barrier, 
build-in moisture if sufficient precautions have not been taken to keep building materials dry during erec-
tion, or by moisture in the outdoor air that is introduced by ventilation. Resent research indicates that the 
infiltration of indoor air is the most significant contributor. (Hagentoft, Sasic Kalagasidis, 2010) 
In a typical household of five persons, 6 kg moisture is produced daily, from moisture producing activities. 
(CIBSE, 2006). Studies have shown that about 20 % to 30 % of this produced moisture enters the roof space. 
(Sanders, Haig, 2004) This happens as a combination of diffusion and air movements, with the latter of the 
two as the dominant, since air leakage through structures carries much more water vapor than diffusion 
through materials. (Essah et al., 2006b). 
Since most of the moisture to an attic room arises because of moist air moving from the inside of the dwell-
ing, it is very important that the construction is tight. Therefore, the Danish Building Regulations allow an 
air change through the building envelope of maximum 1,5 l/s pr. m2 in at pressure test at 50 Pa. (Danish 
Building Regulations 2010 (BR10), 7.2.1) 
1.2. Recommendations cold roof ventilation 
The organization “the Danish Building Research Institute” (Statens Byggeforskningsinstitut, SBi) have create 
a series of publications with directions for technical solutions, which they recommend on basis of research 
and experience from the building industry. In direction no. 224, “Moisture in buildings”, recommendations 
regarding how to avoid moisture damage in constructions are presented. The following is recommended 
regarding cold roof constructions. 
 
Cold roofs are usually made as ventilated constructions, where moist indoor air that enters the 
construction is removed by ventilation on the cold side of the insulation. 
 
The ventilation should include unutilized attic rooms, gaps under diffusion tight roofing underlays, 
gaps between insulation and roofing underlay, and gaps between roofing underlay and roof cov-
ering. 
==PAGE:10==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
9 
 
Figure 1 – Principal sketch of ventilation in a roof construction with cold zones. (Illustration from SBi direction 224) 
 
It cannot be expected that a diffusion open roofing underlay (with Z-value < 3 GPa m2 s/kg) suffi-
ciently can remove moisture from large cold attic rooms (width of floor surface > 1 m). Unless the 
vapor barrier have been made perfectly, “modest ventilation” should thus be established in cold at-
tic rooms with a diffusion open roofing underlay, by for example ventilation valves or gaps for eve-
ry second truss. 
Another Danish organization BYG-ERFA, which collects experiences from the construction industry and pub-
lish these in short informational sheets, presents similar recommendations. In an informational sheet re-
garding ventilation of roof constructions with a large inclination, the following is stated. 
 
Cold attic rooms with a diffusion tight roofing underlay should always be ventilated by openings 
near the eaves and the top of the attic room.  
 
Cold attic rooms with a diffusion open roofing underlay and with a depth larger than 1 meter 
should at least be ventilated with ventilation valves placed alternating in the top and bottom 
for every second truss. 
Recommendations from both organizations agree on that some ventilation should be established in cold 
attic rooms with a diffusion open roofing underlay. The parameter variations in experimental setup of this 
project have been constructed with a distance of 1 meter from eaves to the inner attic wall, in order to test 
the recommendations for large cold attic rooms. 
1.3. Reservations towards recommendations 
Multiple studies of the influence of ventilation in cold attic rooms have shown that the positive effects of 
establishing ventilation openings in vapor open roofing underlays are neglectable or absent.  
In one study, heat, air and moisture tools (HAM-tools) were used to estimate the influence of type of roof-
ing underlay, tightness of the building envelope, insulation thickness and amount of ventilation. The study 
showed that ventilation of loft spaces improves the risk of condensation formation slightly, but not signifi-
==PAGE:11==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
10 
cantly, since ventilation also to some extent increases the moisture loads. The energy losses through the 
ceiling are also increased with increased ventilation. (Essah, Sanders, 2004) 
In another investigation, an extensive case study of Swedish attic rooms with warm chimneys was made 
together with simulations by using HAM-tools. Attic rooms in five houses situated in the Stockholm region 
were investigated by logging the temperature and relative humidity of both air and materials. Some of the 
findings are that the by reducing ventilation in an attic room, the risk of night radiation and condensation at 
the eaves is lower, and that the risk of incoming wind-driven snow is reduced. Reduced ventilation of attic 
rooms, in combination with diffusion open-surface coverage, appears to reduce the risk of moisture and 
mold damage in comparison with traditional vented attics. However, ventilation during the construction 
stage was still found to be necessary. The explanation for the observed effect of ventilation given in this 
study was the following: The temperature in the attics is usually warmer than the outside. The incoming 
cold air normally enters in the lower part of the structure and exhaust in the upper part. Since the attic 
floor usually is above the neutral-pressure plane of the house, the top of the conditioned space is pressur-
ized and these factors drive leakage air into the attic. (Harderup, Arfvidsson, 2013). 
In a third study, the mold growth risk in attic rooms were investigated with respect to the expected climate 
changes in the twenty-fist century, also by means of HAM-tools. Typical attic constructions for domestic 
houses in Sweden were simulated with variations in ventilation design and insulation under the roofing 
tiles. The findings showed that the lowest risk of mold growth was in an attic with mechanical ventilation. 
The highest risk of mold growth was in an attic with ventilation openings along the roof eaves, followed by 
an attic with gable side ventilation. The study also showed that insulation between the underlay and venti-
lation gap, helps improving the moisture conditions. However, if gable ventilation is used in combination 
with insulation of the underlay, any benefit that the insulation could have on the moisture conditions is 
lost, and the mold risk becomes nearly as severe as the attics ventilated by the eaves. (Nik, Sasic Kalagasid-
is, Kjellström, 2012). 
The presented studies are just a few examples that show that there is reason to question the tradition of 
ventilation in cold attic rooms. 
1.4. Investigation questions and methods 
Since large amounts of moisture can be transported to and from attics by air movements, ventilations in-
fluence on the moisture conditions is crucial to improve the custom by which cold roof constructions are 
made today. Therefore, this project will mainly be focusing on how ventilation design influences the mois-
ture conditions, but also how this influence can be explained by an investigation of air movements outside 
attics, to and from attics, and inside attics. 
In the following, research questions will be presented together with the method by which they are going to 
be answered in this project. All questions concerns how moisture conditions and air movements are influ-
enced by the design of the cold attic room and the temperature and wind conditions. 
1. How does the design of a cold attic room affect mold growth promoting moisture conditions? 
2. How much air from the interior enters a cold attic rooms by infiltration? 
3. How much air enters and leaves the cold attic room by roofing underlay valves? 
4. How does the air move inside a cold attic room and how is the temperature and moisture distributed? 
In an attempt to answer these research questions, three different methods are used: 
==PAGE:12==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
11 
1. Full-scale experiment: Most importantly, a full-scale experimental setup of a roof construction with 18 
different cold attic designs is expected to enable direct comparison of different designs. Measurement 
data of temperature and relative humidity is assumed to help answering question 1. In order to find an 
answer question 2, the pressure difference between the inside and the cold attic is measured, from 
which the infiltration flowrate might be calculated. In an attempt to answer question 3, a tracer gas ex-
periment is conducted in order determine the air change in the attic, by which the flowrate through the 
underlay valves possibly can be derived from. Furthermore, the wind conditions are also measured out-
side the experimental setup, which is assumed to be related with the other measured values. 
2. Theoretical model on air movements: In an attempt to underpin the answers for question 1, 2 and 3 a 
theoretical model on air movements in the experimental setup is created. The model is divided into 
some zones where air can flow between. The model is based on mass balance equations and pressure-
loss/mass-flowrate relations. The driving forces are wind and temperature differences. 
3. Computational Fluid Dynamics simulation: The tool pack FLUENT is used for numerical simulation of a 
model of a cold attic room with approximately the same geometry as in the full-scale experiment. By 
dividing the model in to a finite number of elements, and using the governing equations for momen-
tum, energy, pressure and turbulence, the distribution of flow, temperature and moisture is expected 
to be found. Thereby, a qualified estimate on an answer for question 4 would be identified, among the 
other question.  
This report will first concern the three mentioned methods, and present how they are established. Thereaf-
ter, methods for acquiring measurement result from the experimental setup will be presented. In the sec-
tion about results, data from all measurements in the full-scale experiment will be shown, along with the 
predictions from the theoretical model and CFD simulation. Some of this data will be processed and ana-
lyzed in order to find new information, whereupon relations will be outlined and discussed. The conclusion 
will discuss the general trends that can be observed from this investigation, together with some recom-
mendations for minimizing moisture and mold growth in cold attics.

Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
12 
2. Experimental setup 
The experimental setup is located at the test ground 120 of the Department of Civil Engineering at the 
Technical University of Denmark in Kgs. Lyngby, Denmark. (55° 47’ 9.26’’ N, 12° 31’ 23.83’’ E). 
The test facility is constructed from two old reefer containers with a pitched roof constructed on top each 
container. The inclined roof planes both face north and each of the roof constructions have been divided 
into nine separated cold attic rooms. Thus, 18 different variations are investigated, and from measurement 
on these attic rooms and their surroundings, information about how each design performs is gained.  
The current experimental setup is a development of an earlier phase of the same experiment. In phase I, 
only one container was used with eight different variations. Other types of roofing underlay, and valves in 
the underlay were used together with different established leakages between the inside and the attic 
rooms. Otherwise, the base setup have been used for the establishment of both containers in the present 
phase of the experiment. Many of the features in the following presentation of the experimental setup will 
therefore be a repetition of many of the features in the setup of phase 1.1 
2.1. Construction 
In order to make the results from the experimental setup as valid as possible, special attention has been 
given to reduce the sources of error in the experiment. This has been done by making all attic rooms as 
similar as possible and ensure that the construction parts are impermeable to air- and vapor flows, while 
being heavily insulated. Technical drawings of the experimental setup is presented in the following, and 
drawings to scale are presented in appendix a and b. Since the construction of the setup in the two con-
tainers is almost similar, they share the same cross section and floor plans. 
                                                          
1 Setup for phase 1, is described in the MSc thesis “Cold Attic Rooms with Vapor-Open Roofing Underlay” by Christo-
pher Just Johnston (2012).

2.1.1. Container 
Both containers in the setup are reefer containers, which are designed for transporting goods that needs 
constant temperatures. Convector radiators maintain the temperature inside each container at least 20 °C 
and humidifiers keeps the relative humidity above 60%. No cooling or dehumidification equipment have 
been installed, so the indoor environment cannot be kept constant in all seasons where the conditions will 
be higher than the desired. 
The containers inner and outer shell is made of aluminum, with a rigid insulation material in between. On 
the inside the surfaces has been covered with plywood boards, and the containers have been furnished 
with the most basic for an office. The heat and moisture capacity of the interior therefore resembles a 
normal dwelling. 
Fresh air valves have been installed in the north and south facing walls, to avoid negative pressure occur-
ring in the container, with a distance of 0,9 m from the gables and 1,8 m from the ground. The plate valves 
are approximately opened by 12 mm. 
The interior of the container measures (L x W x H) 11,6 m x 2,25 m x 2,3 m. 
The exterior dimensions area are presented in figure 5. 
 
Figure 5 – Exterior dimensions of the experimental setup. The two containers in the setup have the same dimensions.

2.1.2. Pitched roofs 
The pitched roofs on both containers are constructed so the inclined surface faces north, in order to avoid 
the influence of solar radiation on the roof plates. The roof is made as a typical construction where rafters 
make the base structure and laths supports the roof. The roof plates are corrugated fiber-cement sheets of 
the type Cembrit B5, with roofing underlay underneath. The roof is ventilated at the eaves and is equipped 
with ventilation cowls near the roof ridge. On the roof on container B nine cowls are installed, but on the 
roof of container A is only equipped with eight cowls, because of reuse of the setup in phase I. 
Both pitched roofs are connected to their containers by two rectangular openings in the ceiling of the con-
tainer at either end. The opening at eastern end is placed 290 mm from the gable and 360 mm from the 
southern wall and measures (L x W) 750 mm x 560 mm. The opening near the western gable is placed 290 
mm from the gable and 360 mm from the southern wall. This opening is fitted with a hatch with a small fan 
designed to mix the air in the loft corridor with the air from the container.

2.2.1. Infiltration 
An ordinary cold attic room will experience infiltration of indoor air through unintentional leakages to the 
inside. According to the Danish building regulations 2010, the volume flow rate through the building enve-
lope of newly constructed buildings is not allowed to exceed 1,5 L/s pr. m2 heated floor area at a pressure 
of 50 Pa. This is typically tested with a blower door test. For low energy buildings 1,0 L/s pr. m2 is allowed. 
In general, it is difficult to seal structures around joints properly, espe-
cially for joins between the ceiling, wall and roof. In this experiment, it 
is assumed that a large part of the leakage through the construction is 
concentrated at the joint between walls and ceiling structures. Air 
leaked out here is assumed to travel into the cold attic rooms.  
To create intentional air leaks to the attic rooms, PVC tubes have been 
installed to penetrate the container ceiling and the insulation at the 
floor of the cold attic room. By pressure calculations on the tubes, the 
dimensions are determined to the pressure loss over the tubes leading 
to each attic room is as intended. The number and lengths of these 
tubes is presented in table 6 and 7. The calculation procedure is pre-
sented in the section about the theoretical model of airflows to attics. 
It was originally the goal to design for a normal leakage of 1,5 L/s pr. m2 heated floor area. However, due to 
a mismatch between the calculated dimensions of the infiltration tubes in the first phase of the project, 
and the dimensions of the installed infiltration tubes in present phase, the calculated leakage at 50 Pa is 
only 1,07 L/s pr. m2, which corresponds to 100 % in this setup.2 This is considered acceptable, taking into 
consideration that only few actual dwellings will have a leakage of 1,5 L/s pr. m2 just to the attic rooms. 
Furthermore, the required vapor tightness becomes stricter with the new regulations, so the leakages in 
this setup is on the safe side. 
                                                          
2 See appendix l for calculation of leakage.

2.2.2. Roofing underlay 
For the purpose of this project, three different roofing underlays are used. All roofing underlays have been 
placed following strict standard in attempt to keep the construction as tight as possible. 
All attics on container A and one attic on container B, have been constructed with a diffusion tight roofing 
underlay called Monarfol Super from Icopal. This underlay only lets very small amount of vapor pass, which 
is why it also can be used as a vapor barrier other placed in a construction. The purpose of having these 
variations is to find out what influence of the permeability of the roofing underlay has on the ventilation 
and moisture conditions in the attic rooms. 
In container B, all attics except two are made with a diffusion open roofing underlay called Tyvek Pro from 
DuPont Building Innovations. Moisture can escape from these attics, but they will also have faster response 
to high moisture concentrations in the ventilation gap under the roof. These variations are established 
since the investigated recommendation is regarding vapor open roofing underlays. 
One attic in container B have been constructed with a diffusion open roofing underlay called Monarperm 
1000 from Icopal, which was the underlay used in all attics in phase 1. This roofing underlay is covered with 
a water absorbent felt material in order to reduce the risk of drops of condensed moisture. The variation 
replicated in this phase was the one with the highest average relative humidity in the first phase, and is 
included in this setup in order to link results from the two phases.

2.2.3. Underlay valves 
In some of the attics, the roofing underlay have been equipped with valves. These are of the type IcoAir 
from Icopal have an inner diameter of 10 cm leaving an opening area of 78,5 cm2. The flow through this 
type of valve cannot flow out or in perpendicular to the underlay, since a section blocks this direction. In-
stead, the flow has to come from the sides, where grilles prevent dirt and leafs entering the attic rooms.   
 
Figure 7 – Geometry of ventilation valve in roofing underlay 
In some attics, a valve has only been placed in the top of the attic room, to ensure pressure equalization 
with the outside. In other variations, valves have both been placed in the top and the bottom of the roofing 
underlay, so that outside air can enter through one valve and exit through the other. Thereby the two 
valves in combination makes natural ventilation of the attic possible. The pressure equalization valve is 
placed 200 mm from the top of the attic room and the ventilation valve in the bottom is placed 200 mm 
above the insulation on the attic floor. A datasheet for the valve is found in appendix d. 
In two attic rooms with diffusion tight roofing underlay, fans were mounted on the valves in the top and 
bottom, in order to find the influence of having a constant air change rate in an attic room. The fan mount-
ed on the bottom valves blows air in to the attic room, at the same flow rate as the fan in the top blows air 
out of the attic. The fans can be adjusted to different flowrates between 0,18 L/s and 0,7 L/s, corresponding 
to an air change rate of 0,5 hr-1 to 2 hr-1. When the fans were installed, the flowrate was set to about 0,22 
L/s. Since the fans were installed too late in the project, the measurement from these attics have not been 
included in the data analysis, but can be a subject for future investigations. 
From the time the experimental setup was constructed in the current phase, until the fans were installed in 
the two attic rooms, they were equipped with two ventilation valves. This means that in that time two pairs 
of attic rooms had the same parameter variations, which is A8 & A7, and A9 & A3. This data, which theoret-
                                                          
3 The manufacturer does not provide information about air penetration resistance, but this is assumed neglectable 
compared to other flows to and from the attic.

ically should be the same, can be used to identify the influence of the position of the attic room in the set-
up and for investigation of the similarity in construction of these attic rooms. 
2.3. Location of measurement equipment 
There are several measurement devices installed in the experimental setup. Below is an overview of where 
they are placed in and around the setup. 
2.3.1. Temperature and relative humidity sensors 
Two different sensors are used to measure temperature and relative humidity.  
 
HygroClip 2, Rotronic: This device contains both sensors for measuring temperature and relative 
humidity with good precision even in harsh environments. 
 
Thermocouple type T: With this thermocouple, copper-constantan conductors are used to measure 
the temperature at a defined location. 
Each attic room is equipped with three sensors: Two HygroClip 2 sensors and one Thermocouple. 
Each HygroClip 2 sensor is inserted into the attic room by 
a PVC tube that penetrates the back wall of the attic from 
the loft space. The sensors are thereby held in place at 
locations that covers the attic room volume as good as 
possible, where the location is displaced compared to 
each other, being opposite of the positions of the under-
lay valves. 
The sensor at the bottom is 0,39 m from the eastern attic 
wall, 0,3 m from the attic floor and 0,52 m from the back 
wall of the attic. The sensor in the top is 0,39 m from the 
western attic wall, 0,55 m from the attic floor and 0,25 m 
from the back wall.   
A thermocouple sensor has been installed in the center of 
the plane formed by the vapor-open roofing underlay. 
This thermocouple is placed inside an overlap of two ad-
joined sections of the roofing underlay.

2.3.2. Pressure differential sensors 
In order to achieve knowledge about the flowrates in and out of the attic rooms, differential pressure sen-
sors have been installed in seven different attic rooms. The pressure difference over the infiltration tubes 
and valves in the roofing underlay is measured continuously, see table 10. 
The differential pressure sensor used is of the type SDP1000-L025 from Sensirion, since this is a sensor with 
a relatively good precision and range, still having an analogue output, which is used by the logging system. 
Hoses are connected to the pressure sensors while the other end is fixed at locations inside and outside the 
attic, where the pressure difference is measured, see figure 12 and 13. 
Due to the expenses to the pressure sensors, only a few attic rooms of special interest are chosen for inves-
tigation. Three attic rooms with diffusion open roofing underlay and full infiltration are investigated. Sen-
sors are also installed in four attic rooms with a reduced infiltration rate and a diffusion tight underlay.

2.3.3. Wind sensor 
Since wind is a major contributor to the air driving forces inside and around the experimental setup, it is 
important to know the wind conditions locally during the testing. Thereby a relation can be found with the 
wind conditions, and the influence of this determined. 
The instrument used for local wind measurements is an ultrasonic anemometer, WindSonic from Gill in-
struments. By measuring the transit time of ultrasound between two pairs of transducers, both information 
about the speed and the direction of the gas flow is achieved.

2.3.4. Tracer gas experiment setup 
The purpose of a tracer gas measurement is to measure the air change in an air volume, by releasing a 
small amount of gas in one point while monitoring the concentration of this in another point in the volume. 
From this, the flowrates in and out of the volume can be derived. 
In the attic rooms where measurements are made, tubes for dosing and sampling of tracer gas have been 
installed. These are placed with a good mutual distance so short circuit of gas is avoided. The dosing tube is 
placed at the bottom of the room, so that the gas might be spread upwards and mixed, to reach the sam-
pler tube in top. 
 
Figure 18 – Location of tracer gas tubes. 
The tracer gas tubes are placed in four different rooms, two with diffusion open roofing underlay and two 
with a diffusion tight underlay, all in attics where there is underlay valves. 
Table 11 – Attic rooms where tracer gas experiments have been made.

Tubes are connected to the equipment, which is pressurized by the tracer gas. Tubes are placed inside the 
container, so that the dosing point and sampling point is located on opposite ends. Good mixing is ensured 
by placing three fans in the container. 
==PAGE:26==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
25 
Freon 134a is used as a tracer gas, which is a so-called green Freon. This gas is often used as a tracer, it is 
present only at low concentrations in the environment (about 0,2 ppm), it is non-toxic at the concentra-
tions used and it is non-reactive at the temperatures in this experiment. 
 
Figure 19 – Setup of photo-acoustic gas monitor and doser-sampler equipment, connected with tubes.

2.5. Calibration of measurement equipment 
In the following different attempts to measure the accuracy of the instruments are presented. From this 
information, the accuracy of the measurements can be ensured, and sometimes improved. 
2.5.1. HygroClip 2: Temperature and relative humidity 
The HydroClip2 sensors were calibrated in May 2014, in a climate chamber with a controlled relative hu-
midity and temperature. The relative humidity in the climate chamber was controlled with a saturated salt 
solution to settle at a relative humidity of 33 %, 75 %, 93 %, and 97 %. By making linear regression for the 
relative humidity indicated by the sensors and the actual relative humidity in the chamber, calibration for-
mulas were derived. 
However, it turned out that when calibrating data 
with this method, RH measurements seemed unrelia-
ble, since they in general became higher and sur-
passed 100% humidity more often. On average, with-
out calibration the measurements were over 100 % 
RH, 9 % of the time, while having values above 100 % 
RH, 10 % of the time with the calibration. Without the 
calibration, the highest humidity recorded was 100,3 
%, which is within the expected accuracy of the in-
struments. With calibration the highest humidity was 
108,5 %, which is far beyond what can be the explained by instrument inaccuracy. 
Since the calibration does not seem to benefit the accuracy, it was decided not to use it on the data. The 
sensors should be re-recalibrated another time to ensure the accuracy of the HydroClip2 sensors. This be-
Figure 20 – Climate box for calibration of HydroClip2 sensors. 
==PAGE:28==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
27 
comes more important in time since there might be a long-term shift in measurement, which is < 1 % RH 
and about 0,1 °C pr. year. The present influence on the measuring results is assumed within the accuracy of 
the measuring instruments. 
2.5.2. SDP1000-L025: Pressure difference 
The sensors uses a five-volt input current. The transformer that supplies this current is adjusted manually 
to be as close to the correct current as possible, but lacks precision. Since the sensors are very sensitive to 
any deviation from the correct input current, the output signal might have a systematic error because of 
the input current.  
A series of measurements where no pressure difference should be measured, were therefore executed. No 
tubes were connected to the sensors, so the sensors should ideally have an output current of 2,1 V corre-
sponding to zero pressure difference. In table 16, the results of a measurement with no tubes connected to 
the sensors are presented.

2.5.3. WindSonic: Wind speed & direction 
The anemometer used in the experiment, had unfortunately been damaged at some time, and the support-
ing construction was slightly bended. Therefore, a calibration was executed using a wind tunnel. A stand 
had to be made for the anemometer, so that it did not move even at high wind speeds in the wind tunnel. 
The wind speeds was measured accurately by a Coobra Probe, which measures the air pressure very accu-
rately around the probe head and converts this into a wind velocity. The frequency of the Coobra Probe 
was set to 500 Hz, which is high compared to the frequency of the anemometer of 1 Hz. The probe head 
was placed facing the wind direction, in front of the anemometer. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
                                                          
4 See conversion formula, equation 116, in section about the pressure sensors.

The calibration was made by using the wind tunnel in building 119 at the Technical University of Denmark, 
Kgs. Lyngby, Denmark. This wind tunnel can generate wind speeds from 0 m/s to 30 m/s, and is equipped 
with a rotating bench for testing the subject from multiple angles. 
 
Figure 23 – Wind tunnel with anemometer and stand for Coobra Probe. 
In total 121 test rounds were conducted, one for calibration of the Coobra Probe and for all combinations 
of every 30º turn and 10 different speeds in the range 2 m/s to 30 m/s. Every test ran for about one minute, 
where data was collected from the anemometer together with data from the Coobra Probe, if a series of 
tests with a new velocity was initiated. The average value the readings in each test is used as a result. The 
calibration results are presented in the following graphs for correction of the wind measurements, where 
each curve describes a test series with constant velocity.

From figure 25, the wind angle seems to be overestimated by the anemometer. This error is not much in-

The error of the measured wind angle and wind speed measured by the anemometer are both a function of 
the actual wind angle and wind speed. Therefore, the measurement data from the anemometer cannot be 
corrected directly, but through two iterations where the corrected values from the first calculation are used 
as inputs for the next.

3. Theoretical model of airflows 
This section is concerning a theoretical model of the air movements in the attic rooms, the container and 
the roof itself. The purpose is to develop a method to predict the flowrates in and out of an attic room, and 
compare these results with the measured air movements for validation. 
The developed model is based on the experimental setup, which has been divided into five different zones, 
with 11 flows of air considered between the zones.5 
 
Figure 26 – Principal sketch of zones, flows and heights in the theoretical model. Flows to other rooms are not shown here. 
                                                          
5 A theoretical model for the setup of phase I is described in the MSc thesis “Cold Attic Rooms with Vapor-Open Roof-
ing Underlay” by Christopher Just Johnston (2012). Since many elements of this experiment is the same, there will be 
similarities in the method. 
==PAGE:33==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
32 
3.1. Temperature in zones 
Since this theoretical model only concerns air movement, the temperatures at the different volume zones 
have been assumed constant at a given temperature. These temperatures are seen in table 17, and are 
based on an approximated yearly average.

3.2. Pressure loss calculation 
In order to calculate the air flows in the construction, it is important to know how the mass flow rate is 
influenced by the pressure differences before and after the obstacle for the air movement. This is depends 
on the geometry of the obstacle and the surface properties. In the following, equations used for the pres-
sure loss calculations will be presented, thereafter the pressure loss of the openings in the experimental 
setup will be calculated. 
3.2.1. Theory on pressure loss calculation 
All pressure losses in the construction is calculated from two types of pressure losses – friction losses and 
local losses.  
A local loss occurs when the cross section area of the airflow changes.6 
 
∆𝑝𝑙𝑜𝑐= 𝜉∙1
2 ∙𝜌𝑎∙𝑣2 
 
(1) 
where  
ξ is the local loss coefficient [-] 
ρa is the density of the air [kg/m3] 
v is the average velocity in the cross section [m/s] 
 
The local loss coefficient depends on the obstacle type. For these calculations, two types are needed, as 
presented in table 18. 
Table 18 – Local loss coefficient due to a change in cross section. 
Type of cross section change 
Local loss coefficient 
Entrance 
0,5

3.2.2. Connection tubes 
For flows up to 1,7 L/s, which in the connection tubes corresponds to a velocity of 2,4 m/s, the flow here is 
laminar. For the purpose of this project, a laminar flow in the connection tubes is assumed. For a laminar 
flow the total pressure loss is calculated as.

by the prefix of the pressure difference.

3.2.3. Vapor open roofing underlay 
Two types of roofing underlay is used in the experimental setup; one that is open for diffusion and one that 
is diffusion tight. The mass flow rate of air through the diffusion tight roofing underlay is considered ne-
glectable, while the airflow through the diffusion open roofing underlay is calculated from information 
from the manufacturer. 
The area of the roofing underlay in one attic is: 
 
𝐴𝑣𝑜𝑟= √(1,015𝑚)2 + (1,015𝑚)2 ∙1,26𝑚= 1,809 𝑚2 
 
(9) 
The amount of air transported through the underlay depends on the resistance to penetration to air, which 
is given from a pressure test at 50 Pa. (See appendix h). This is converted in to a form that is useful in the 
calculations. It is assumed that the flow is outwards, so that the density of the temperature in the attic 
room is used.

3.2.4. Ventilation valves in container 
The two ventilation valves in each container consist of an outer wall grid, a hole through the container wall, 
and an inner inlet/outlet. The pressure loss of the grid and the inlet is know from the data sheet from the 
supplier. (See appendix e and f) The hole in the wall is calculated as a friction loss over a pipe. 
 
Figure 27 – Container ventilation outer grid and inner inlet/outlet. 
The pressure loss of the outer grid is found from the following diagram in the datasheet. The diameter of 
the grid is 100 mm. 
 
Figure 28 – Pressure loss diagram for the outer grid in the container wall. 
From figure 28, the following expression can be derived. Note that the expression is SI-units, m3/s, while 
the diagram is not.

be calculated directly.

3.2.5. Ventilation valves in attic 
The pressure loss of the ventilation valves in the roofing underlay is approximated by calculating the local 
losses from the different parts of the valve. The valve can be subdivides into two volumes, with tree local 
losses and one friction loss. 
Along the ridge of the ventilation valve is a number of grooves that the air passes through.

3.2.6. Gaps over and under laths 
The laths are mounted on top of the rafters with a thin counter-batten in between. On top of the laths, the 
conjugated roof plate is placed. This creates a series of narrow gaps between the laths and the roof plates, 
and laths and roofing underlay, where the air flows through in order to get from the eaves to the roof cowl 
on the top of the roof. 
 
Figure 30 – A view on the roofing underlay from the inside, with one 
roof plate removed. One lath is seen in the middle, and on top and 
bottom of this, a small gap where air can flow through. 
The roof is of the type Cembrit B5, which is a conjugated plate of a cement-based material. The geometry 
of the plates given in the product datasheet is approximated with a sine curve for further calculation.

3.2.9. Remaining attic rooms 
The airflow through the infiltration tubes to the remaining attic rooms is important to include in the model, 
since it otherwise would overestimate the infiltration airflow to the attic room considered. 
==PAGE:47==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
46 
This airflow is estimated from the expected pressure loss between the infiltration tubes and the outside. 
 
∆𝑝𝑟𝑒𝑠= ∆𝑝𝑡𝑢𝑏𝑒.𝑟𝑒𝑠+ ∆𝑝𝑣𝑒𝑛𝑡.𝑡𝑜𝑝.𝑟𝑒𝑠+ ∆𝑝𝑐𝑜𝑤𝑙.𝑟𝑒𝑠 
 
(76) 
The number of remaining airflow obstacles depends on the room that is under consideration. The number 
of airflow obstacles for the room under consideration, should be subtracted from the total number of air-
flow obstacles for that container. In this case, for the sake of simplification, the pressure-flow relation to 
other attics is assumed the same in all cases. The number of infiltration tubes, top ventilation valves and 
cowls are assumed to be the following, corresponding to the attics in B with B6 subtracted.

3.3. Driving pressures 
The pressure differences in the setup is what causes the air to move, therefore called driving pressures. The 
two main contributions are the stack effect and the wind pressure on the outside. 
3.3.1. Stack effect 
The stack effect is a result of buoyancy, which depends on the density and vertical position in the air or 
fluid. Pascal’s law, also called the hydrostatic law, describes this the following way. 
 
Δ𝑝= 𝜌𝑎∙𝑔∙Δℎ 
 
(82) 
From this, the pressure of the stack effect is known, when using a reference pressure. 
 
𝑝𝑠𝑡𝑎𝑐𝑘= 𝑝𝑟𝑒𝑓−𝜌𝑎∙𝑔∙ℎ 
 
(83) 
In order to use the hydrostatic law some assumptions have to be made, to ensure that the density of air is 
known. In the model, a uniform temperature distribution in each environment is assumed. Furthermore, 
the air is considered incompressible and thus has a constant density within one zone. 
3.3.2. Wind pressure 
The wind velocities and directions measured by the anemometer on top of the setup are used for calculat-
ing the corresponding pressures at openings. From this, a relation between the wind and the airflows might 
==PAGE:48==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
47 
be found. The wind pressures are calculated according to the standard for wind calculation, DS/EN 1991-1-
4.  
The terrain category in the area around the setup is chosen in accordance with an assessment of the local 
conditions, as presented in table 20. 
Table 20 – Terrain categories and roughness heights.

z0 [m]

1

4. Computational Fluid Dynamics simulation 
In the following, a numeric model using the Computational Fluid Dynamics (CFD) package FLUENT is pre-
sented. The purpose of this simulation is to achieve insight in the causes for the observed results in the 
experiment, by analyzing the air movements inside a cold attic room by a numeric simulation. 
4.1. Geometry 
When modelling a complex reality it is important to simplify the case as much as possible, in order to re-
duce computational time, ease the mesh generation, and reduce the risk of errors. Therefore, only the 
most important features of the construction have been included. Only one attic room is modelled, together 
with a section of the ventilation gap between the underlay and roof plates. The geometry is simplified to be 
a triangle prism with an extra layer on the sloped side.  
In the cavity between the roofing underlay and the outer roof surface, four rectangular volumes are sub-
tracted. These are the laths in the roof construction, which hinders a direct flow from the eaves to the top 
of the roof, and makes the flow more turbulent. Therefore, these are important for the simulation. 
 
Figure 38 – Geometry of modelled attic with shaded exterior. 
Inside the volume, the roofing underlay is made as a surface that separates the ventilation gap and the 
attic. Two holes are placed near the corners of the surface, which represents the valves in the underlay. 
Near these openings, circular plates are placed at some distance. This is to model a simplified version of the 
underlay valves, where air cannot enter from the front but only from the sides. At the attic floor, a circular 
inlet is placed to simulate the infiltration. The cross section area of the inlet is the same as the total cross 
section area of the infiltration tubes in the experimental setup. However, the hydraulic diameter is set to 
correspond to one infiltration tube.  
==PAGE:54==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
53 
 
Figure 39 – Wireframe of modelled attic with tops of underlay valves and leakage inlet highlighted. 
The dimensions of the geometry are as presented in table 25.

4.2. Mesh 
The calculation method works by dividing the volume into a finite number of elements, which can affect 
their nearest neighbor elements, in terms of energy, momentum, temperature, pressure and moisture con-
tent. There are two types of mesh that are typically used in this kind of simulation: Tetrahedron or hexahe-
dron mesh. Hexahedron mesh is based on cubes and is often used for geometries with many right angles. In 
this case, tetrahedron mesh is used, since this mesh is based on triangular prisms and the base geometry of 
the attic room is triangular. 
In total, the geometry is divided into about 570000 elements, by automatic generation. Mesh controls are 
used for refinement at certain sensible locations where the gradients are steeper. These are mainly near 
walls, inlets and outlet. They are presented in table 26. 
==PAGE:55==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems

4.3. Solution method 
The method used by FLUENT for solving a problem depends very much on the case, since a method good 
for one thing is not necessarily useful in another case. This is because the discretized version of many bal-
ance equations is used, since they in their original form would be too time consuming to solve completely. 
The methods used in this case are often used for simulation of indoor air movement, because of the rela-
tively low velocities. These are presented in table 27.

4.4. Boundary conditions 
The boundary conditions are all surfaces in the model that in combination influences the conditions and air 
movements inside the volume.  
4.4.1. Conditions at neighbor zones 
In the simulation, three different zones next to the attic room are considered. They are the inside, the out-
side and the neighbor attics. Inside and in the neighbor attics the conditions are considered constant in all 
cases, while simulations with different conditions outside are investigated. Three different seasons are in-
vestigated, in order to analyze the influence on the attic room at different times of the year. 
The first season represents the average outdoor conditions during a year. The second is the warmest sea-
son, where the temperature is assumed the same as inside the dwelling. Thereby the influence of tempera-
ture differences in the boundary conditions are disregarded. The third season is the coldest monthly aver-
age during the year. The humidity of the outdoor air is calculated as the average of all loggings with ±0,5 °C 
of the temperature in consideration. The boundary conditions are presented in table 28. 
 
 
 
Table 28 – Boundary zone conditions.

4.4.2. Outer surfaces 
Different types of outer surfaces are used: 
 
Air is let in to the volume at the bottom of the ventilation gap, by two velocity inlets above and be-
low the first lath. The velocity is considered constant here, assuming that the conditions does not 
vary from case to case. The volume flow rate is estimated based on the pressure loss in the eaves, 
and the pressure generated by wind on a calm day. In order to have conditions that generates an 
inflow to the volume and not an outflow, wind is considered to come from the north with a velocity 
of 0,5 m/s. 
 
The top surface of the ventilation gap is assigned to be an outlet. Here air is blown out if there is 
overpressure and is sucked in, in case of negative pressure. 
 
At the bottom of the attic floor, a circular area is assigned to be a pressure inlet. This works just like 
an outlet. The air that is sucked in has some assigned properties in terms of temperature and hu-
midity for the indoor air. Air enters when there is a negative pressure near the opening in the attic. 
In the experimental setup, there might be an overpressure inside the container, due to wind condi-
tions and buoyancy forces. However, all infiltrations in this CFD simulation is considered a result of 
air flows and pressures in the attic rooms only, in order to ensure that assumptions regarding the 
pressure in the container does not influence the conclusions. 
 
The sides of the ventilation channel are assigned to be symmetry faces. Thereby the program 
knows the channel continues to the sides, and flows going near this surface are being perfectly re-
flected. 
 
The rest of the surfaces are assigned to be walls. These surfaces slows down flows in their proximi-
ty, because of the boundary layer of air. The velocity gradient depends on the roughness factor. 
Heat is also transported from the neighbor zones, depending on thickness of the wall and heat 
transfer coefficient. 
The settings for the outer boundaries are presented in table 29 and 30.

Sides of the attic are considered to be insulated, even though they are not in the experimental setup. This is 
to reduce the influence of the uncertainty of the temperature in the neighbour attics. Since the final tem-

4.4.3. Inner surfaces 
The inner surfaces of the model separates different volumes, and are all calculated as walls. These walls are 
considered as planes, thus they do not have thickness. Therefore, only surface properties have to be de-
fined, and not material properties like heat transfer coefficient, as they are considered only to make a hin-
drance for air streams and not conductive heat transfer.

4.5. Calculation convergence 
FLUENT solves the balances in the room volume by calculating how the elements affects each other. First, 
the solution is initialized and all elements are assigned a value. Then by a number of iterations the solution 
approaches to be more and more convergent, which means that the difference between the input and out-
put values for one calculation becomes small enough be considered insignificant. 
 
Figure 44 – Scaled residuals of a calculation, example of a converged solution. 
==PAGE:60==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
59 
5. Data processing and experiments in setup 
This section will describe how information have been gathered from the experimental setup. Data from the 
sensors that have been described earlier is processed and further investigations and experiments are made. 
5.1. Data processing 
5.1.1. Temperature and RH measurements 
5.1.1.1. 
Signal conversion 
The output signal from the HydroClip2 sensors is an analogue signal ranging from 0 to 1. In order to convert 
this signal to useful measurements of relative humidity and temperature, the following relations are used: 
 
𝑅𝐻 [%] = 𝑅𝐻𝑠𝑖𝑔𝑛𝑎𝑙∙100 
 
(102) 
 
𝑇 [℃] = 𝑇𝑠𝑖𝑔𝑛𝑎𝑙∙100 −40 
 
(103) 
In order to ensure that RH at no time surpasses 100 %, RH is set to 100 % if the measurement is more than 
100 %. The output from the thermocouple at the roofing underlay does not need to be converted, as it 
gives the correct output in °C. 
5.1.1.2. 
Calculation of absolute moisture content 
From the temperature and relative humidity, the absolute water content is calculated by using the follow-
ing relations. (SBi-anvisning 224 (2009)).

5.1.1.3.

From the measurement data of temperature and relative humidity, the mold growth potential have been 
predicted from a mold model. In this case, the VTT model is used. (T. Ojanen, H. Viitanen et al., 2007) The 
mold index indicates the amount of mold growth, both microscopic and visual on a qualitative scale, as 
presented in table 32.

5.1.2. Wind measurements 
The data from the anemometer is generated with a frequency of 1 Hz. In order to make use of this data it 
has to be converted into 10-minute intervals like the rest of the measurement data. This is done by taking 
an average of all wind vectors during a 10-minute period. First, every vector is dissolved into an x and y 
==PAGE:63==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
62 
corposant. Thereafter the average of the x and y corposants is converted back to an angle and speed. 
Thereby one average is found for every interval, which do not only considers the angle and speed as sepa-
rated values, but as one unit.  
 
Figure 45 – Principal sketch of vector averaging used for finding average wind speed and direction. 
The corposants are found the following way. 
 
𝑥= 𝑣∙sin (𝜃)

5.2. Tracer gas experiment 
Another way to achieve knowledge about the volume flow rate through the valves in the roofing underlay 
than measuring the pressure difference, is to make a tracer gas measurement. In the present case, a guard-
ed tracer gas experiment was conducted by making use of the constant concentration method. It works by 
releasing a small amount of gas into a room and constantly monitoring the concentration by a photo-
acoustic gas monitor. Based on the previous reading a new portion of gas is released, which is either small-
er, the same, or larger than the one before. The equipment continues this way, until a steady state is 
reached, where the concentration in the room is stable. When this steady state is reached, and is stable for 
some time, the air change can be calculated. 
Different approaches were used for finding the air change in the attic rooms and from this, the flowrate 
through the underlay valves. At first the aim was to keep a constant concentration is both the container 
and the cold attic room. By this the indoor air would be marked and the tracer gas that would have to be 
==PAGE:65==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
64 
replaced by the dosing unit, would be a direct representation of the incoming outdoor air and information 
about the infiltration air flow would not be needed, for finding the flowrate through the bottom valve. 
However, the problem about this method is that the concentration easily becomes too high in the attic, 
because of the gas coming from the container. The dosing unit then cannot dose, since the target concen-
tration is maintained by itself. Making use of this method only succeeded in one attic room. 
As an alternative, two other approaches were used. One where the concentration is kept constant in the 
attic room and one where the concentration is kept constant in the container. A target concentration of 8 
ppm have been used both places. 
The challenge of the first approach is the very small volumes of air in the attic rooms, since balancing the 
concentration of tracer gas then is almost impossible. The dosing unit used can only dose a certain mini-
mum amount of gas, but even the smallest amount can cause the concentration to rise to very high levels. 
This high concentration drops suddenly, when the natural ventilation in the attic room removes gas. This 
results in a concentration that fluctuates, and never reached a steady state, which is an assumption for this 
type of experiment. The precision of this method is thus not very good. In order to get an average that is 
not too affected by outliers, the median value is used instead of the mean. 
 
Figure 46 – Fluctuating concentration in tracer gas experiment with constant concentration in the attic. 
If the concentration is kept constant in the container instead of the attic, the concentration is much more 
stable, since the volume of the container is much larger than an attic room. Since all infiltration air now is 
marked with a tracer gas at a known concentration, the air change of the attic room can be calculated 
knowing the estimated infiltration volume flow rate by the pressure sensors. 
 
Figure 47 – Concentration in tracer gas experiment with a constant concentration in the container. 
When running an experiment with constant concentration in the container, steady state is awaited where 
there is no significant changes in concentration. Thereafter measurements runs for about 45 minutes to an 
hour. The duration is a balance of having enough measurement point to make an accurate average, and 
==PAGE:66==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
65 
having a period with steady boundary conditions. If the experiment runs for too long, the conditions might 
change and the assumption of a steady state is not fulfilled.  
 
Figure 48 – Principle sketch of a guarded tracer gas experiment with constant concentration in the container. 
In case the concentration is constant in the attic, the airflow out is simply derived from the air change, that 
is calculated by the photo-acoustic gas monitor. When the concentration is kept constant in the container, 
the airflows are calculated knowing the constant concentration in the container, and the resulting concen-
tration in the attic. 
 
𝑄𝑜𝑢𝑡= 𝑛∙𝑉= 𝐶𝑐𝑜𝑛∙𝑄𝑖𝑛𝑓
𝐶𝑎𝑡𝑡𝑖𝑐
 
 
(117) 
 
𝑄𝑖𝑛= 𝑄𝑜𝑢𝑡−𝑄𝑖𝑛𝑓 
 
(118) 
where  
n [hr-1] is the air change known from the photo-acoustic gas monitor 
V = 0,65 m3 is the volume of the cold attic rooms. 
Ccon is the average concentration of gas in the container [ppm] 
Cattic is the average concentration of gas in the attic [ppm] 
 
For the one attic room where an experiment with a constant concentration in both the container and attic 
room was successful, the flowrate in through the bottom underlay valve is calculated from knowing the air 
change. 
 
𝑄𝑖𝑛= 𝑛∙𝑉 
 
(119) 
A number of assumptions are needed for the above shown methods to be successful. These are the follow-
ing: 
==PAGE:67==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
66 
 
Infiltration flowrate: If constant concentration method is not possible in both the container and 
the attic, information about the infiltration flowrate is important to derive the flowrate into the at-
tic room from the air change. 
 
Flow direction: Another assumption is that the flow directions are as in figure 48, since it cannot be 
measured in this experiment whether the air moves out of the bottom or the top, but only the 
amount of air. Nevertheless, from simulations, the flow direction is known theoretically and from 
an assumption of air balance in the attic room, the flows are calculated. 
 
Other flows are neglectable: Flows to and from other attics, unintentional leakages and air infiltra-
tion through the roofing underlay are considered insignificant. For a case with constant concentra-
tion in the container, flows through other leakages to the inside will also be marked with tracer gas, 
and thus resulting in a higher concentration. This could cause the calculated flow rates to be small-
er than it really are. The opposite is the case when the concentration is constant in the attic room, 
since more gas would escape or be replaced by unmarked air, resulting in higher flow rates. 
Measurement were carried out during two different outdoor conditions. Two days with much wind and rain 
with an average wind velocity of 3,3 m/s, and one day where there was only little wind and no rain with an 
average wind velocity of 1,5 m/s. 
5.3. Mold sampling 
By data from the temperature and relative humidity sensors in the cold attic rooms, it is possible to make 
an estimation about the potential for mold growth using a theoretical model. However, in order to know if 
this is in accordance with the actual conditions, samples from the attic rooms are needed. Tests were taken 
in seven different rooms, where work also had to be done by installing pressure sensors.  
Multiple method were used for assessing where there have been the most mold growth. At first when the 
attic rooms were opened, a visual inspection was made. The only places where mold was visible in some of 
the rooms, was at the sides of the room, the underlay and the wooden surface in the lowest corner near 
the roofing underlay. Photos of the most covered area 
at this surface are taken. 
Another method used is to take a swab test of an area 
with a visible colony and distribute on a growth medi-
um, whereupon the mold is cultivated in a controlled 
environment. 
A third, much more precise method, is to make use of 
an air sampler in the middle of the room. The equip-
ment makes 100 L of air flush over a medium for 
growth, by which mold spores in the air is collected. 
Thereby the results are directly comparable, since the 
amount of mold sampled is mostly affected by the 
number of spores in the air and very little by human 
interaction. The mold is also cultivated in a controlled 
environment in this case. 
Two different mediums were used in the search for different mold species. DG18 agar is used for mold that 
grow under dry or semi-dry conditions, while V8 agar is used to find more water active species. The mold 
Figure 49 – Air sampling device for mold detection. 
==PAGE:68==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
67 
samples where set aside in a climate chamber for maturing in about two weeks under growth favorable 
conditions. 
 
Figure 50 – Example of matured mold samples from attic A5. The samples are from left to right: Air sample DG18, air sample V8, 
swab sample DG18, swab sample V8. 
 
 
==PAGE:69==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
68 
6. Results 
6.1. Temperature and RH measurements 
Measurement are available in one full year from July 9 2014 until of July 10 2015, only with a few intermis-
sions because of experiment modification or electrical cuts. These intermissions are mainly in July and Au-
gust 2014, since there were some problems with data collection in the startup phase of the project. Unfor-
tunately, it has not been possible to collect data from the rest of July and August 2015, in order to have 
data from a full year without intermissions, since data had to be processed in time for the deadline of this 
report.  
In order to get an overview of how the conditions are at different times during the year, the average 
monthly measured values are presented for different locations in the following. For sake of simplification, a 
full year is presented as going from January to December, even though measurements were gathered from 
July 2014 to July 2015. 
6.1.1. Boundary conditions 
The boundary conditions are is environment surrounding the attic rooms. An assumption for the experi-
ment is that the boundary conditions are alike, since a potential difference between conditions in the attic 
rooms then can be assigned to a parameter change, and not the boundary conditions. The attic rooms all 
face an outer environment on the inclined side with roofing underlay and roof plates, an inner environment 
at the back and the bottom of the room, where insulation and moisture barriers have been placed, and 
other attic rooms at the sides, which are separated by wooden plates with moisture barriers on one side. 
These barriers between the boundaries and the attic rooms delays the response of a boundary condition 
change.  
6.1.1.1. 
Outside and under roof 
The outside variations in relative humidity and temperature is presented in figure 51.

values close to 100 % in the early morning. The daily variations are larger during summer than during win-
ter, since solar radiation has a larger influence on the outdoor temperature.

of the year, except in July where there is a lower humidity than normally.

than container A at every month.

Figure 53 – Average monthly temperature of outdoor conditions. 
From figure 53, the measured temperature outside is observed to follow the expected temperature fairly 
well, except that the average temperature in July is much higher than expected, and than the months be-
fore and after. This is because of some unusually high measured temperatures in July 2014. The tempera-
tures in July 2015 was what could be expected. The temperatures under both roofs are in general lower 
than the outside especially during in the spring and autumn, which might be a result of not having direct 
solar radiation on the north faced roofs, while having it in the surroundings.  
Another reason for this could be the delayed response there is under the roof due to the connection with 
the attic rooms. When the outdoor temperature rises during the day, the attic rooms functions as a tem-
perature buffer, so the roof volume is cooled down by cold air from the attics.

6.1.1.2. 
Inside variation 
In each of the containers, humidifiers have been installed in order to create the humidity conditions that 
would be expected in a household, while electrical radiators with thermostats are set to keep the tempera-
ture above 20 °C. A ventilator in the ceiling hatch mixes the air so conditions in the container and the loft 
space are approximately the same. 
 
Figure 55 – Average monthly relative humidity of indoor conditions. 
In figure 55, the relative humidity in the interior environment of both containers is observed to be close to 
constant during the first half of the year, while increasing until around September-October and then drop-
ping again until December. Since the humidifier only can adjust the humidity if it is too low, this higher hu-
midity can be expected if the exterior conditions also have a high humidity. Another contributing factor 
could be that the infiltration air backflows into the interior from a moist and cold attic room.  
It is observed that the relative humidity is higher in container B than A. This will influence the results, but 
since this project is mainly focused on the difference between the parameter variations, this will not have 
significant influence on the conclusions drawn. The difference in indoor climate mostly has influence on the 
comparison between the only attic room with a diffusion tight roofing underlay in container B, and the attic 
rooms in container A. The relative humidity in this attic room in B tends to be higher than it would be if it 
were located on the container A. Therefore, results indicating that attic rooms on top of container A is 
more moist than on top of container B, will not be affected by this deviation.

seen to be unusually high in the month of July.

6.1.2. Conditions in attics 
In this section, an overview of the conditions in the attics is presented. As an example of the unfiltered 
measurement data from the attic room air, the conditions in an attic room with vapor open roofing under-
lay, no roofing underlay valves and full infiltration are here presented. 
 
Figure 58 – Unfiltered measurement data at top of attic room B4. 
The temperature variation is seen in figure 58 to be alike the outdoors, which can be expected for all cold 
attics that are affected more by the outside than inside temperature wise. The relative humidity also fol-
lows the same pattern as the outside, even though this has much smaller fluctuations during a short time. 
Even a roofing underlay that can be considered as diffusion open causes a delay in the moisture transport, 
which stabilizes the moisture conditions in the attic room.

==PAGE:75==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
74 
 
Figure 59 – Average monthly relative humidity in cold attic rooms with diffusion tight roofing underlay. 
In figure 59, it is seen that most of the attics with diffusion tight underlay, has a humidity above 95 % in the 
winter and in a part of the spring. The attic room with the highest humidity during the year is room B9, 
which has no infiltration nor ventilation. This variation causes a very slow response to the outdoor condi-
tions, so it will be the last to dry out in the summer, but also the last to become moist in the winter. B9 is 
thus not completely airtight, since it in the end does respond to the seasonal variation. 
The variation with the lowest moisture concentrations is attic A1, which has no infiltration and two valves 
in the roofing underlay. With this variation, the average relative humidity will never be as high as in the 
other attic rooms and have a quicker decline during the dry-up period in the spring.  
Two pairs of attic rooms had been constructed alike, and the data should therefore be alike. This can be 
used to investigate the influence of the attic room location in the setup and the quality of the workman 
shift. For the pair A7 & A8, which has 20 % infiltration and two underlay valves, the humidity profiles are 
close to each other but not completely alike, since the average relative humidity is a few percent higher in 
A8 most of the year. For the pair A3 & A9 with full infiltration and two underlay valves, the profiles follows 
each other well except in the autumn where the humidity in A3 is higher than in A9. 
The observed variation between the rooms, that have been constructed alike, indicates that the investigat-
ed parameter variations are not the only thing to influence the humidity conditions in the cold attic rooms. 
One thing that could influence the humidity is the conditions in the neighbor rooms. A3 is located next to 
some attic rooms that have a relatively high humidity unlike A9, which is placed next to a relatively dry attic 
and the gable. A7 and A8 are placed next to each other, so in this case small differences in tightness of the 
construction is more significant. Despite these uncertainties, general tendencies can still be drawn from the 
data, since the differences are considered small. Attic room A3 and A7 are used for further analysis.

According to figure 61, the moisture content in the attics is highest in July and lowest in the winter months. 
The variations with the highest moisture content are those with no valves in the roofing underlay, and the 
lowest are with valves. When underlay valves are used, a smaller infiltration area results in a lower mois-
ture content. When no valves are used, a larger infiltration area results in a lower moisture content. This 
can seem contra logical, but can be expected for the attic rooms with a higher moisture content than the 
inside. 
 
Figure 62 – Average monthly relative humidity in cold attic rooms with diffusion open roofing underlay. 
The general relative humidity profile during the year for attic rooms with a diffusion open roofing underlay 
is observed in figure 62, to be alike to those with a diffusion tight roofing underlay. In the first part of the 
year until July the attic rooms will dry up, while building up moisture in the last part of the year. 
The attic rooms with the highest relative humidity are in B8, B5 and B6, all having full infiltration. The dry-
up period during the spring is slower compared to the rest of the rooms, while also having the highest max-
imum relative humidity. B8 has a vapor-open roofing underlay and two underlay valves. However, the va-
por diffusion resistance is higher than the rest of the diffusion open underlays, thus letting less moisture 
through. It can therefore be expected that this perform worse than the other attics with a vapor open roof-
ing underlay. B5 only has a pressure equalization valve, while B6 has two underlay valves. B5 has the high-
est relative humidity, especially during the spring. The attic room with the lowest average relative humidity 
is B1, which has no infiltration nor underlay valves. The attic room with full infiltration, with the lowest 
average relative humidity is B4, which has no underlay valves.

6.1.3. Comparison with phase one 
The parameter variation B8 was made as an attempt to make a link between earlier phase one and the 
present phase two of the project, by duplicating the design of the attic room that was concluded to be the 
worst performing attic room in the first phase. 
The variation duplicated from phase one has full infiltration, which in this phase was made with PVC tubes 
with an internal diameter of 15,9 mm. The roofing underlay is diffusion open, but still more diffusion tight 
than the diffusion open underlay used elsewhere in phase two. In both phases, this attic is equipped with 
two underlay valves. However, in phase one the underlay valves were just tubes penetrating the underlay, 
while the geometry of these is more complicated in phase two. 
In the following, monthly average values are presented for comparison of the corresponding parameter 
variation in the two different phases. Data from phase one is collected during more than two full years, 
while data from phase two only have been logged during one full year. One of the humidity sensors in A6 
from phase one was malfunctioning, so only data from the other sensor in this room is presented here. 
Along with data from A6 in phase one, which had two underlay valves just like the attic room B8 from this 
phase, comparison is also made with A5 from phase one, which had just one underlay valve in the top.   
 
Figure 65 – Comparison of temperature in attic room in phase 1 and phase 2.

According to figure 65, the temperatures are in general about the same in both phases. The room in the

July. There is less difference in temperature between the top and bottom of the attic in phase two than in 
phase one. 
Having one underlay valve in the top of the attic room in the variation A5 in phase one, in general results in 
some lower temperatures than the variation with two underlay valves from phase one, which is A6. 
 
Figure 66 – Comparison of relative humidity in attic room in phase 1 and phase 2. 
According to figure 66, the relative humidity on the attic room B8 in phase two, is in general higher than 
the comparable variation A6 from phase one. Especially during the last months of spring is there a noticea-
ble difference. 
The relative humidity in the variation A5 from phase one, with only a pressure equalization valve in the top, 
is observed to follow the same pattern as A6. However, during all months, the average relative humidity is 
systematically higher in the top of A5 than the top of A6. This means that in this case, having one valve in 
the top of the underlay can be expected to cause a higher relative humidity in the top of the attic room, 
than having two valves. 
In order to find out if the observed difference between the phases is due to variation in design and con-
struction of the attics, or because of different boundary conditions, the temperature and relative humidity 
outside and inside is also compared.

6.2. Wind measurements 
In figure 71 and 72, wind measurements from the period from June 16 2015 to July 22 2015 are presented. 
 
Figure 71 – Wind direction as measured by the installed anemometer. 
 
Figure 72 – Wind speed as measured by the installed anemometer. 
According to figure 72, the wind speed is measured most often to be between 0,5 m/s and 3,5 m/s. The 
highest winds speeds are on the dates July 6, 9 and 10, where the wind speed sometimes is more than 4 
m/s. According to figure 71, the wind direction is most often from the west, but fluctuates at some days 
between west and east. From July 1 until July 5, the direction is mostly from the east.

From the visual inspection, it is observed that there in general is more mold growth in attics on container A 
than in attics on container B. There is almost no growth in B4, while there is about the same in B5 and B6. 
In A6, there is only a small amount of growth, while there is heavy visual growth in A4, A5, and A7. It is 
worth noticing that the growth follows the patterns of the grains in the wood. 
The samples from both air and colonies on surfaces showed that there is plenty of mold, actually a bit too 
much to identify species and categorize the growth. However, genus as Penicillium and Cladosporium were 
identified in all attics where samples were taken. Many species from Penicillium are very common in indoor 
air, while most of the spores in outdoor air are from Cladosporium. 
Ranking the test samples from the most mold growth to the least, results in the order presented in table 
41.
