The air and mold samples appears not to be in complete accordance with the visual inspection in container 
A. Here the inspection showed that there should be least mold growth in A6. However, samples from this 
attic turned out to have one of the highest amounts of mold growth. This indicates that visual inspection is 
not a completely accurate measure for mold growth, since not all mold growth might be visual at the sur-
face, but only present further inside the materials. 
For a diffusion open underlay, the worst situation made with only pressure equalization in the top of the 
attic, while having two underlay valves makes it better. The situation with least mold growth is with no 
underlay valves. When the underlay is diffusion tight, having no underlay valves creates the worst situation, 
while having two valves makes it better. Ten percent infiltration is a little worse than twenty percent. This 
might be because these attics sometimes have a moister air than the indoor air, and more infiltration 
therefore will lower the moisture content. 
6.6. Theoretical model 
The results that appears from balancing the theoretical model as much as possible, are presented in figure 
76. 
==PAGE:88==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
87 
 
Figure 76 – Flow rates as predicted by model with best solutions. 
From figure 76, it is observed that a higher wind velocity in most cases causes a higher infiltration and flow 
through the top underlay valve. On the contrary, the flow rate is decreased at the bottom valve when there 
is more wind. The influence of the wind is seen to be most significant when there is only a pressure equali-
zation valve in the attic. 
With regard to the infiltration, having no valves causes the lowest flow rate, since air in the attic room only 
can leave through the underlay. If a pressure equalization valve is used, the airflow through this is the same 
as the infiltration flow. If a valve is placed both in the top and the bottom even more air flows through the 
top valve. However, most of this air comes from the bottom valve, since the pressure resistance is smaller 
here than by the infiltration tubes. This results in the infiltration rate to the attic room being less than half, 
when two valves are placed instead of one. In case of a reduced infiltration area, the flowrate is not propor-
tionally reduced as expected, but only to about 60 % of full infiltration.  
6.7. CFD simulation 
Twelve different CFD simulations have been made varying the infiltration inlet size, the number of ventila-
tion valves, and the outdoor temperature and relative humidity. 
The simulations does not have air or moisture diffusion through the roofing underlay, but only transport of 
heat. Results should therefore be in coherence with the measurements in attics with a diffusion tight un-
derlay, since CFD simulations model conditions in such attic rooms. 
6.7.1. Plots

ture distribution, relative humidity, and moisture content fraction. All plots are taken from three vertical 
sections in the attic room. These sections are located in the middle of the bottom underlay valve, the infil-
tration opening and the top underlay valve. Only a few chosen plots of special interest from the simulation 
are presented in this section.

6.7.1.1. 
Two roofing underlay valves 
6.7.1.1.1. 
Yearly average outdoor conditions 
 
Figure 77 – Yearly average outdoor conditions – full infiltration and two underlay valves – vector plot of section in middle of bottom 
ventilation valve. 
As seen in figure 77, outdoor air enters into the volume below and above the lath near the eave. The air 
entering at the top of the lath does not follow the roof for long, but falls down where it moves into the attic 
room at a high velocity through the bottom valve. A constant circulation along the attic walls and underlay 
is present counter clockwise in the plot, since air is heated up by the back wall and cooled down at the roof-
ing underlay. 
Air coming from underneath the lath can enter directly into the attic room, since the underlay valves are 
open in the sides. After air have entered with a velocity of about 0,15 m/s it moves along the attic floor. 
The rest of the air that does not go into the attic room moves along the outer surface of the underlay, 
where this is heated a little by the attic room, and gains speed due to buoyancy. 
==PAGE:90==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
89 
 
Figure 78 – Yearly average outdoor conditions – full infiltration and two underlay valves – vector plot of section in middle of infiltra-
tion leakage. 
According to figure 78, the air that moves along the bottom of the attic room causes a slight overpressure 
here, which makes the infiltration flow negative. Thereby attic air, which have been infected with mold 
toxins, might enter the dwelling where it can cause health issues. However, this effect might not be ob-
served in reality, because of a potential overpressure in the dwelling, which have been disregarded in this 
simulation.  
 
Figure 79 – Yearly average outdoor conditions – Full infiltration and two underlay valves – vector plot of section in middle of top 
ventilation valve. 
==PAGE:91==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
90 
According to figure 79, air top of the attic room is sucked out because of negative pressure arising from air 
movement in the ventilation gap. Since the cross section area is decreased here, the velocity increases. 
Because of the design of the ventilation valve, air is pressed to the sides creating turbulence and a vortex 
under the roof. Most of the air leaves over the top of the lath, near the top of the attic room. 
 
Figure 80 – Yearly average outdoor conditions – Full infiltration and two underlay valves – temperature contours of section in middle 
of bottom ventilation valve. 
In figure 80, cold air is seen drop down from the ventilation valve in the lower corner of roofing underlay. 
Inside the attic, the cold air is transported along the attic floor. Further op in the attic room a good stratifi-
cation is observed, where air that is heated up by the slightly warmer walls moves upwards to the top. 
==PAGE:92==

Figure 81 – Yearly average outdoor conditions – full infiltration and two underlay valves – temperature contours of section in middle 
of top ventilation valve. 
In the section presented in figure 81, the air heated slightly up by the attic leaves through the top valve. 
Some of the heat also distributes further down the ventilation gap.

6.7.1.1.2. 
Summer situation 
 
Figure 84 – Summer outdoor conditions – Full infiltration and two underlay valves in summer – velocity vectors of section in middle 
of underlay valve in bottom. 
In the summer situation, as seen in figure 84, there is no buoyancy to force the air in the ventilation gap, in 
to the attic room. Instead, the air moves along the inner surface of the roof plates. The flow will naturally 
distribute, which causes some air to be pressed below the laths, each time the air stream meets a lath in 
the ventilation gap.  
 
Figure 85 – Summer outdoor conditions – full infiltration and two underlay valves in summer – velocity vectors of section in middle 
of infiltration leakage. 
==PAGE:95==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
94 
A slight overpressure is generated in the ventilation gap, which results in a net flow to the attic room from 
both the bottom and the top valves. In order to maintain a mass balance, the infiltration direction becomes 
out of the attic room into the dwelling, as observed in figure 85. 
6.7.1.1.3. 
Winter situation 
 
Figure 86 – Winter outdoor conditions – Full infiltration and two underlay valves in winter – velocity vectors of section in middle of 
underlay valve in bottom. 
In the winter situation as presented in figure 86, some of the flow patterns observed at the average tem-
perature are reproduced. The cold air drops down through the bottom underlay valve, at even higher 
speeds than at a higher outdoor temperature. This causes larger amounts of air to enter the room, which 
lies as a cold blanket on the attic floor.

tive infiltration flow rate, as observed in figure 87.

6.7.1.2.1. 
Yearly average outdoor conditions 
 
Figure 89 – Yearly average outdoor conditions – full infiltration and one underlay valve – velocity vectors of section in middle of 
infiltration leakage. 
From figure 89 it is observed that in case only a pressure equalization valve has been installed in top of an 
attic room, the infiltration air enters at high velocity in case of the average outdoor conditions. Because of a 
negative pressure in the ventilation gap compared to the attic room, air is sucked out of the pressure equal-
ization valve. To maintain mass balance, a corresponding amount of infiltration air enters through the leak-
age to the dwelling.

In the summer situation where the outdoor temperature is the same as the indoor temperature, the situa-

thereby an infiltration flow into the dwelling.

6.7.1.2.3. 
Winter situation 
 
Figure 93 – Winter outdoor conditions – full infiltration and one underlay valve in winter – velocity vectors of section in middle of 
infiltration leakage. 
The winter situation presented in figure 93, is again much alike the situation with average outdoor condi-
tions. The airflow in the ventilation gap causes in combination with buoyancy forces, high amounts of air to 
be sucked through the pressure equalization valve. To compensate for this, air flows through the leakage, 
but at even higher rates this time, since the temperature difference between the infiltration flow and the 
rest of the attic room air is larger. 
==PAGE:101==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
100 
6.7.1.3. 
No roofing underlay valves 
6.7.1.3.1. 
Yearly average outdoor conditions 
 
Figure 94 – Yearly average outdoor conditions – full infiltration and no underlay valves – velocity vectors of section in middle of 
infiltration leakage. 
According to the presented figure 94 of a simulation without underlay valves, air circulation still takes place 
in such an attic. This is mainly because of temperature differences between the cold roofing underlay and 
the warmer interior walls. This also enables some air to enter the attic room by infiltration, if the same 
amount also leaves, to keep a mass balance. However, since in the beginning the air entering the cold attic 
room is moister than the air leaving, moisture is build up in the attic room until the moisture content is the 
same as inside the dwelling and a balance is achieved. This situation is therefore very risky, when having a 
diffusion tight roofing underlay, since it allows moisture to accumulate. 
6.7.2. Air flows in and out of attic room 
From the CFD simulation software, FLUENT, it is possible to gain information about volumes and flows at 
boundary conditions and at internal openings. These results are presented in the following diagrams, where 
positive flow direction have been defined as presented in figure 95. 
==PAGE:102==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
101 
 
Figure 95 – Definition of positive flow direction in CFD simulation results. 
 
Figure 96 – Volume flow rates in CFD simulations given different designs and conditions. 
In figure 96, the flowrates to and from the modelled attics are presented, for a different number of under-
lay valves, and a different amount of infiltration, during three different temperature seasons. The variation 
with 20 % infiltration have only been simulated for the yearly average outdoor temperature. 
The volume flow rate of infiltrating air is observed to be positive in case there is only a pressure equaliza-
tion valve in top of the attic. Most of the air that leaves through the top valve has to come from the infil-
trating air to keep a balance in the room. 
This is different in the case that there are two underlay valves, where the infiltration is negative in all situa-
tions. An overpressure is generated at the bottom of the attic room, because of the large flowrate through 
the bottom valve. In reality, this pressure might be smaller than the pressure in the interior, because of 
overpressures generated by wind, but such driving pressures in the container have been disregarded in this

==PAGE:103==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
102 
simulation. Therefore, the infiltration could be positive in some occasions even though this is not showed in 
this simulation. 
The flowrate through the top valve is larger when there is two valves in the underlay, since much of the air 
comes from the valve in the bottom, which drives the flows in the attic room. With only one valve, the max-
imal possible flow through this valve is determined by the infiltration opening. 
The temperature of the inlet air affects the flowrates. When two underlay valves are used, having colder 
weather increases the flowrate through the valves. The infiltration flowrate becomes more negative in cold 
weather, because of high pressures at the attic floor. In warm weather, the infiltration is also negative be-
cause air flows into the attic room through both the top and the bottom valve. 
In case only one valve is used, the flowrates are a little higher in the cold season, since the temperature 
difference between the infiltration air and the attic room is larger. In the warm season, the flowrates are 
slightly negative, since buoyancy does not drive the infiltration air upwards anymore. 
In case the infiltration area is reduced, the infiltration respectively out and in of the attic, is seen to be pro-
portionally reduced too. Furthermore, a relation between the infiltration flow and the flowrate through the 
top valve is observed. For an attic with two valves, when the negative infiltration is larger, the flow though 
the top valve is smaller. For an attic with one valve, the flow through the top valve becomes larger with a 
larger infiltration flow. 
6.7.3. Conditions in attic 
 
Figure 97 – Mean temperature in attic rooms of CFD simulation. 
In figure 97, the influence of the numbers of ventilation valves, and the amount of infiltration is seen to 
only have a small influence on the mean temperature in the attic room. The temperature in the attic rooms 
is close to the outdoor temperature, since the air moves along both sides of the underlay, which has little 
insulating effect. In a situation with average temperature and during the cold season, the temperature in 
the attic is lowest when there is two valves, since there is a higher air change. The warmest temperatures in 
these periods is when there is only pressure equalization, since infiltration air heats up the attic room air.

In the presented CFD simulation, the ventilation design that resulted in the highest relative humidity, and 
therefore also the design the can cause the greatest moisture problems, was the variation with no roofing 
underlay valves. Here infiltrating moisture is trapped in the attic room where condensation becomes likely 
especially in the winter, due to low temperatures. 
The second worst situation is in case just a pressure equalization valve is placed in the top of the attic 
room. In this case, during most of the year, large amount of infiltration air enters the attic, but there is also 
much moisture removed. This still causes condensation in the cold season, but not for a situation with the 
average outdoor temperature. In the warm season, the flow is reversed, making the conditions in the attic 
close to the outside. 
The ventilation design that causes the least moisture problems in the attic is with two underlay valves, 
since moist air is quickly removed by ventilation, which leads to an attic room that is close to the outdoor 
conditions.  
When considering that the simulated attic rooms have a diffusion tight underlay, the results are in good 
coherence with the measurements in the setup, where the attics with the most moisture problems were 
==PAGE:106==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
105 
the ones with no valves and the ones with the least problems had two valves. No parameter variations have 
been made in the experimental setup with a diffusion tight underlay and just one underlay valves, but if it 
were made, the moisture performance of this room should, according to this simulation, be somewhere in 
between the other variations.

Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems

7. Analysis 
7.1. Temperature and RH measurements 
7.1.1. Condensation at roofing underlay 
From the calculated moisture content in the attic rooms and the measured temperature of the roofing un-
derlay, it is calculated if there is condensation here. This data is used as an important comparison condi-
tion, since liquid water might accumulate in the attic room and should therefore be avoided. 
 
Figure 100 – Risk of condensation on diffusion tight roofing underlay. 
Condensation is seen in figure 100 to be a common problem, with only August having no risk of condensa-
tion in any of the attic rooms. The risk of condensation peaks during the winter months, where there is 
condensation all the time in some of the rooms.  
Just like with the comparison of relative humidity, four attics distinguish themselves by having a slower 
decay in risk of condensation during the spring. These attic rooms have no underlay valves, and therefore 
responds less to the outdoor conditions. However, during the autumn, the attic rooms with the highest risk 
of condensation are the ones with underlay valves, since they have faster response to the outdoor condi-
tions. Since this moisture build-up period is shorter than the dry-up period, this will have less influence on 
the average risk of condensation during a full year.

Figure 101 – Risk of condensation on diffusion open roofing underlay. 
According to figure 101, when a diffusion open roofing underlay is used, the risk of condensation is less 
than with a diffusion tight underlay. The three summer months are all without any condensation. In the 
other seasons, the risk is highest for the attic rooms with underlay valves and full infiltration, with conden-
sation all the time in the winter. The medium risk during the late autumn, winter and early spring, is for all 
attic rooms with either underlay valves or full infiltration. The risk for condensation in an attic room with no 
infiltration nor underlay valves is very low during the winter.

ing underlay.

7.1.2. Mold model 
By applying the described mold model to the data from the attic rooms, the mold growth development is 
calculated during one full year. The end mold index in July 2015 is used at the initial mold index in July 
2014. In periods with mission data, the mold decay or growth is assumed to continue at the same rate as 
the last calculated.  
 
Figure 103 – Predicted development mold growth index in one-year period. (See appendix n for a larger version of the figure.) 
In figure 104, the mold index is seen to rise very sudden in the end of august 2014. In some rooms, the 
mold index continues to rise, until it reaches the maximum possible coverage. For other rooms the growth 
is halted before this takes place. When the time is reached, the mold index drops with a steep decline, so 
that the growth potential goes from between five and six to zero, during the curse of some months. A full 
dry-out of mold during the one year is predicted in all attic rooms. 
Because of some interruption in the measurements in July and August 2014, data is missing here and ex-
trapolation of the mold growth index is therefore used. This mainly influences the mold growth index in this 
period, since the mold growth index will grow slower anyway in September and October when it approach-
es its peak value.

Figure 104 – Time at mold index 3 to 6. 
Summarizing for a mold index in the high part of the index during one year, figure 104 is generated.  
The risk of mold growth is in general highest for a diffusion tight roofing underlay, combined with no valves 
in the underlay. In this case, the amount of infiltration does not have a significant influence. When underlay 
valves are used, the risk of mold growth is always lower, but is also more sensitive to the amount of infiltra-
tion.  
With a diffusion open roofing underlay, the highest risk of mold growth is found in attics with only one 
pressure equalization valve in the top, thereafter in attics with two underlay valves. The lowest risk of mold 
growth is in attics with no underlay valves. More infiltration is observed to cause a higher risk of mold 
growth in all cases, with attics with no underlay valves being the most sensitive. 
When comparing the predictions of the mold model with the mold that was actually sampled from the set-
up, there is a good coherence. The order of which the samples from the attics were classified is almost the 
same, except that A6 is predicted to be worse than A4, which was observed to be the opposite when sam-
pling. In general, there is a good agreement, which indicates that the mold growth model can be used for 
making accurate predictions for comparing mold growth risks in other cases, given that there are reliable 
temperature and RH measurements.

Figure 105 – Wind rose of wind measurement from 16/6-2015 to 22/7-2015. 
The wind rose in figure 105 describes average wind speed and wind directions for the period in considera-
tion. For example, it is observed that more than 35 % of all measurements comes directly from the west, 
with almost half of this having a wind velocity above 2 m/s. For wind speeds lower than 1 m/s, it is seen 
that the wind is mainly from the east. However, reservations toward this should be taken, since measure-
ments of direction is unsure for lower wind speeds, and because of the fact that the anemometer is not 
calibrated for such low wind speeds. 
In order to validate the wind measurements, so that they may be used for further analysis, the generated 
wind rose is compared to with the average monthly standard normal (years 1961 to 1990) near that loca-
tion. The Danish Metrological Institute administers the official weather station 06160 FSN VÆRLØSE. Data 
from this station have been used to generate the wind roses in figure 106 and 107. (DMI, Technical Report, 
99-13)

The general pattern of the wind rose generated from data from the anemometer looks like the climatic

of the very stormy weather on the 9th and 10th of July. Wind speeds are in general higher at the DMI weath-
er station, which might be because it is located out in open terrain, while the experimental setup is located 
in a sub-urban area. The measurements form the anemometer are therefore considered valid.

In figure 108, the error lines to the left indicate the measuring inaccuracy, which is calculated from the 
standard deviation of the measurement with no hoses connected. An inaccuracy of two standard deviations 
is used, corresponding to a pressure of about ±0,05 Pa from the average. The error lines to the right is the 
maximum error as stated in the datasheet of the pressure sensors. This has a constant error of ±0,31 Pa, 
which is converted to a mass flow rate error. 
By measurements made at zero pressure difference, the maximum variation in the output signal was found 
near zero. This is assumed to describe the random error that occurs because of variations in the supply 
current, the electronics in the sensors, and the connections to the sensors. The other type of error is a con-
sequent error in the signal that diverge from the signal, which should be produced at a certain pressure. 
The accuracy described in the data sheet is assumed to describe the maximal potential combined error of 
any sensor of that type.

The accuracy is considered fair for most pressure measurements at the infiltration openings, and can there-
fore be used for further investigation. However, the accuracy is unacceptable for the pressure measure-
ments at the underlay valves. This is because errors are scaled up to become larger because of the relative-
ly large opening area of the valves. Therefore, tracer gas measurements are therefore used instead to gain 
information about the flowrate here.  
The accuracy is not very good either at the infiltration openings at B4, B5 and B6, since they all have a rela-
tively large infiltration area. However, this causes greatest relative error by the infiltration at B4, since this 
also has a low average flowrate. Data from these sensors is however used, but one should bear in mind that 
measurements with only small variations might be somewhat unsure. 
On average, the infiltration flow rate to the two attic rooms with underlay valves is indicated to be higher 
than the infiltration to an attic room with no valves, which is as expected. No significant difference can be 
observed between the infiltration to attic rooms with one and two valves. 
At attic rooms with a reduced infiltration area and two valves, the flow is also reduced compared to that for 
full infiltration. For the attic rooms with no valves and a reduced infiltration area, the infiltration rate is 
further reduced. When comparing attics with a reduced infiltration leakage with attics with full infiltration, 
the measured reduction in the flowrate is not as large as expected. This could indicate that there are some 
other air leakages to the room than through the infiltration tubes, since the relative influence of the pres-
sure resistance at the infiltration tubes then would be of less significance. 
 
 
==PAGE:115==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
114 
8. Discussion 
8.1. Temperature and RH measurements 
8.1.1. Parameter comparison 
For analyzing the measurement data that have been outlined in the previous section, different parameter 
variations influence on the measured values are compared. As a tool for comparison, the absolute change 
in percent time in a considered interval shows what effect a specific parameter change has, all other thing 
equal. For example, an increase in time at the considered interval means that the investigated parameter 
makes it more likely that the measured physical property of the attic is higher, compared with an attic 
where this parameter is not present. A comparison is made with attic rooms with no infiltration tubes or no 
underlay valves. 
Error lines shows the influence of the measurement error introduced by the sensors in the setup. The high-
est possible deviation is in each case calculated by subtracting the time in interval in the lowest measure-
ment error margin for an attic with no change, from the value in the highest measurement error margin for 
a room where one parameter variation have been made, and vice versa. 
8.1.1.1. 
Influence of infiltration of indoor air 
First, the influence of infiltration is analyzed separately by comparing attic rooms with no ventilation, but 
with different infiltration opening size. 
 
Figure 109 – Infiltrations influence on the time at a critical relative humidity. 
From figure 109, the introduction of a small infiltration in observed to cause a small increase in time with a 
critical relative humidity, in attic rooms with diffusion tight roofing underlay. However, full infiltration is 
observed to cause a slight decrease in the relative humidity. This can either be a result of infiltration back-
flow to the container, or be because the reference attic with no infiltration has a higher moisture concen-
tration than the container. Infiltration of less moist air could then lower the humidity.

When a diffusion open roofing underlay is used, infiltration undoubtedly causes more hours with a critical 
relative humidity. The relative larger influence of infiltration compared to attics with a diffusion tight roof-
ing underlay, could be because air leaves through the diffusion open roofing underlay. In order to keep a 
mass balance, moist air can enter through infiltration openings to the container. 
 
Figure 110 – Infiltrations influence on the time at a high temperature. 
According to figure 110, the time in the high end of the temperature range, which is considered to be 
above 15 °C, rises due to additional infiltration in case a diffusion tight roofing underlay is used. Very little 
variation between the different amounts of infiltration is seen, indicating that this only has little influence 
when no underlay valves are used.  
In case a diffusion open roofing underlay is used, the time with high temperatures decreases in the bottom 
of the room because of more infiltration. This could seem contra-logical, but might be because warm air 
from the infiltration opening stimulates the air circulation in the room, and thereby enables more cooled 
air from the roofing underlay to fall to the attic floor.

Figure 111 – Infiltrations influence on the time with a high moisture content. 
In figure 111, high values are considered to be above 9 g/m3, based on the typical range of the moisture 
concentration. As expected, infiltration is observed to have a general tendency of increasing the time with a 
high moisture concentration. Only a relatively small difference is observed between the different amounts 
of infiltration, indicating that the infiltration flowrate stabilizes at a certain level, independent of infiltration 
area.  
There is a small tendency that the change in hours with a high moisture content is highest in the top of the 
room, even though this is not completely sure because of the inaccuracy. However, this would make good 
sense since the moist and warm air from the inside, naturally will rise to the top. The change for an attic 
with a vapor open roofing underlay is a bit smaller, which can be because the moisture is able to escape 
from this attic room.

According to figure 112, there is a general tendency that more infiltration causes more time with condensa-
tion. However, this change is not very large for a diffusion tight underlay, since there already is much con-
densation without any leakage in the vapor barrier. For an attic with a diffusion open roofing underlay, the 
influence is more evident. Here more infiltration clearly causes more condensation on the roofing underlay. 
8.1.1.2. 
Influence of type of roofing underlay 
In the following, the relative influence of a different roofing underlay is investigated. The reference is in all 
cases the diffusion open roofing underlay Tyvek Pro. The diagrams show the change in time at a certain 
interval, by changing from this underlay to another. 
 
Figure 113 – Roofing underlay’s influence on the time with a critical relative humidity. 
According to figure 113, a more diffusion tight roofing underlay causes in general more hours with a critical 
relative humidity where mold growth is possible, especially when no underlay valves are installed. The in-
fluence is worst for attic rooms without underlay valves, since the moistures only mean of escape to the 
outside is to diffuse through the underlay. If the underlay is diffusion tight, the moisture is trapped.  
One exception for an increase in relative humidity due to a more diffusion tight underlay, is an attic room 
with no infiltration and two underlay valves. Here there is a tendency that having a diffusion tight roofing 
underlay improves the moisture conditions in top of the room. This indicates that there are some situations 
where a diffusion tight underlay might perform better than a diffusion open underlay. However great care 
should be taken, since infiltration can cause a diffusion tight underlay to perform worse than diffusion open 
underlay. 
When comparing with the diffusion open underlay used in phase one of the project, which is more diffusion 
tight than the reference underlay, the increase in time at a critical relative humidity is higher than a corre-
sponding attic with a diffusion tight underlay. This observation might be because of the moisture absorp-
tive properties of this exact roofing underlay.

In general, the variation with two valves and no infiltration was the only case where having a diffusion tight 
underlay did not make the moisture conditions worse in the attic room. This variation has the lowest 
amount of moist air coming from the inside, combined with the best possibility of getting rid of moisture by 
ventilation. Apparently, the underlay valves fulfills the need for an escape of moisture is this case. There-
fore, the diffusion tightness of the underlay is not important for the moisture balance. In the other cases, 
there is either too much moisture infiltrating or too few openings for the moisture to escape the attic 
room, so having a diffusion open roofing underlay causes an improvement. 
8.1.1.3. 
Influence of insulation on roof plates 
On top of the roof plates, 50 mm polystyrene insulation have been mounted in front of an attic with no 
underlay valves and full infiltration. This is to reduce the potential influence of night radiation, and find out 
if this initiative can improve the moisture conditions in a cold attic. This attic room is compared with an 
attic room that is constructed alike in every way, except that it does not have insulation on the roof plates. 
 
Figure 118 – Roof insulations influence on time with high relative humidity. 
In figure 118, the insulation on roof plates is observed to a have a small tendency to reduce the time with 
high relative humidity in the bottom of the attic room. In the top, there is no difference. 
 
Figure 119 – Roof insulations influence on time with high temperature.

Figure 122 – Roof insulations influence on time with high risk of mold growth. 
According to figure 122, the insulation on the roof plates only causes a relatively small reduction in risk of 
mold growth in the bottom of the room, while the reduction in top of the attic room is insignificant. This 
can be expected, since it was in the bottom that a small reduction in time with a high relative humidity was 
observed. Stratification due to buoyancy most likely plays a role here, since cold air near the inner surface 
of the underlay, tends to fall to the attic floor, where it increases the relative humidity. If the number of 
hours with low temperatures at the underlay is reduced, the risk of having low temperatures at the attic 
floor is also reduced.

From figure 123, additional valves are observed to reduce the number of hours with a critical relative hu-

there is full infiltration. In this case the infiltration of moisture is simply too large to be removed completely 
by ventilation, and therefore it does not make a big difference whether valves have been installed or not. 
 
Figure 124 – Underlay valves influence on time at a high relative humidity for a diffusion open underlay. 
When the roofing underlay is diffusion open, the underlay valves causes more hours with a high relative 
humidity, according to figure 124. Using one valve in the top of the roofing underlay causes a higher rela-
tive humidity, than when having them both in the top and in the bottom. This effect is most significant 
when there is full infiltration.

the time with high temperatures is increased for an attic with one valve, while there is a tendency to a de-

When there is no infiltration, placing two underlay valves reduces the moisture content in the air, according

small reduction in the moisture content. For 100% infiltration, the moisture content is not considerably 
influenced when placing underlay valves.  
 
Figure 128 – Underlay valves influence on time at a high moisture content for a diffusion open underlay. 
According to figure 128, the underlay valves causes a small increase in the moisture content, even when 
there is no infiltration, for a diffusion open roofing underlay. The worst case is when there is full infiltration 
combined with only one ventilation valve in the top of the roofing underlay. This indicates that a significant 
amount of moisture infiltrates from the inside in this situation. The situation is a better when there is two 
ventilation valves. For both amounts of infiltration, it is observed that for attics with two ventilation valves, 
there is a tendency to have a smaller raise in the moisture content in the bottom than in the top. This is 
opposite for an attic with one valve and full infiltration. 
8.1.1.4.4. 
Condensation at roofing underlay 
If the relative humidity becomes high, condensation might occur on cold surfaces. It is assumed that the 
roofing underlay most often is the coldest surface in the attic room, since this is convectively cooled by 
outdoor air passing by. Therefore, by knowing the condensation profile of the roofing underlay, a good 
indication of the overall moisture condensation in the rest of the room is gained.

Diffusion tight: In all cases, ventilation valves improves the moisture conditions, since it lowers the mois-
ture content. This results in a lower relative humidity and ultimately a lower risk of mold growth. When the 
infiltration is high, underlay valves does not help as much as when the infiltration is smaller. 
Diffusion open: Ventilation valves in the underlay causes in all cases a higher risk of moisture and mold 
growth. When the infiltration is low, underlay valves are likely to result in a higher increase in the risk of 
mold growth than in case of full infiltration. One underlay valve is more likely to result in a higher increase 
in relative humidity than two underlay valves. 
8.1.2. Data regression 
Analysis of time in interval gives a good qualitative impression on the relation between the parameter vari-
ations. However, one limitation is that there is no information about which outdoor conditions that causes 
the greatest differences in the conditions in the attics. Such information would be relevant in order to know 
how the performance of a design is affected by weather conditions. 
A relation between all the different attic rooms and the conditions under the roof is approximated in terms 
of temperature and moisture content of the air. A non-linear regression using least squares method is used 
to find a relation. The purpose of this is to tell in general terms, under which conditions valves in the roof-
ing underlay diminish or exacerbate moisture related problems and how much can be expected. 
==PAGE:130==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
129 
A useful relation have been found between the conditions in the attic rooms with vapor open roofing un-
derlay and the conditions under the roof. An R-squared value of 0,99 is achieved for all relations except for 
one, where the R-squared is 0,95. This indicates a good relation between the data. In order to have an idea 
of the precision of the predictions, the upper and lower bound is presented in the following diagrams as 
thin lines. This is a combination of two standard deviations and the maximum measuring inaccuracy. The 
two standard deviation covers 95,5 % of the data, which means that the conditions in an attic room are 
very likely to be within the bounds, given a known condition under the roof. 
 
Figure 133 – Tendency lines of influence on moisture content, by having one additional underlay valve, given there is no infiltration 
for a diffusion open roofing underlay.  
From figure 133, it is seen that there is a slight negative relation between moisture under the roof and the 
absolute change in moisture content by in installing one valve in the roofing underlay instead of having no 
valves. This can be interpreted in the following way: During the months with a low moisture content in the 
air under the roof, the air in the attics is also relatively dry. Therefore, any moisture escaping from the in-
side to the attic room is more significant, since the same amount of moisture can cause a relatively larger 
increase in the average moisture content in the attic. Even though the attics have been constructed using 
the strictest standards for airtightness, this shows that there is some air infiltrating from the inside, and 
that this is affected by having pressure equalization valves. The change in moisture content is most likely to 
be largest in the top of the room, which could be because the pressure equalization valve causes a mixing 
of the attic room air, by which moisture is transported to the drier top.

Figure 137 – Tendency lines of influence on moisture content by having one additional underlay valve, given there is full infiltration 
for a diffusion open roofing underlay.  
According to figure 137, having pressure equalization valve is likely to increase the moisture content in the 
attic room, especially if the outdoor moisture concentration is low. For a moisture contents under the roof 
being lower than about 7 g/m3, the valve cause an increase with about 70-80 % probability. This increase, 
like in the case with no infiltration, can be explained by moist infiltration air enters the attic because of the 
pressure equalization. The relative influence of the moisture entering is more significant if the moisture 
concentration is already low. 
The relation between the data is unfortunately not as good in the present case, as it is for some of the oth-
er relations, since the R-squared is not as high. Therefore, the probabilities cannot be stated exact, but the 
observed tendencies can still be trusted.

Figure 138 – Tendency lines of influence on temperature by having one additional underlay valve, given there is full infiltration for a

additional heat comes from the infiltration air, which heats up the attic volume especially in the winter. 
In the warm period, there is little difference between an attic with pressure equalization and one without. 
This could indicate that the infiltration to the two attics is the same, and that the influence of the pressure 
equalization valve has been minimized.

Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems

Figure 140 – Tendency line of influence on temperature by having two additional underlay valves, given there is full infiltration for a 
diffusion open roofing underlay.  
The two underlay valves are seen in figure 140 to cause a temperature increase in the cold season and a 
temperature decrease in the warm season. It might seem contra-logical that ventilation air can cause 
warmer temperatures, but this can be explained by that the ventilation causes more infiltration air to enter 
the attic. If the temperature under the roof is low, the relative warm infiltration air increases the tempera-
ture. During the warm season, the infiltration air is relatively cold compared to the attic room air, and the 
temperature will be decreased with more infiltration air. 
In general, the valves in the roofing underlay was not observed to have any significant influence on the 
moisture or temperature conditions in an attic room with no infiltration. The exception was the influence 
on the moisture concentration when installing a pressure equalization valve in the underlay, which caused 
higher concentrations especially in dry weather. 
When there was full infiltration, a pressure equalization valve in the roofing underlay caused a higher mois-
ture concentration in most cases, except in the moistest seasons, while causing an increased temperature 
in the cold seasons. The influence of the having two underlay valves is smaller than for a pressure equaliza-
tion valve. Again, the increase in moisture concentration was highest in the dry seasons. The temperature 
was decreased at high outdoor temperatures and increased at low outdoor temperatures. This could indi-
cate that infiltration air influences the temperature.

Figure 144 – Average infiltration flowrates for a wind from direction 300°, west-northwest. 
In case of wind direction west-northwest, the wind only has little influence with speeds at 0 to 3 m/s, as 
presented in figure 144. Higher wind speeds are observed to cause a higher infiltration flow rate for attics 
with full infiltration. For attics with a reduced infiltration, the high wind speeds causes the infiltration rate 
to become negative, meaning that the infiltration flow is from the attic room to the inside. 
8.3. Tracergas experiment 
In figure 145, the results from the tracergas experiments with constant concentration in the container are 
compared. Results from the pressure measurements at the infiltration tubes at the time of the experiment, 
are also included for easier comparison.  
 
Figure 145 – Volume flow rates in and out of four attic rooms, according to tracer gas experiment with constant gas concentration 
in container. 
According to figure 145, the flowrates through of the underlay valves are in general indicated to be higher 
when there is much wind than when there is less wind. One exception is A5 with a diffusion tight underlay, 
two valves and 10 % infiltration, where the measured flowrates through the valves are unexpectedly low in 
case of windy weather. This might not be a good representation of the average conditions during a longer 
time, but only be some special flow conditions right at the moment of the tracer gas experiment. This 
measurement is therefore not considered reliable. 
For a diffusion open roofing underlay, the flowrate through the underlay valves in top is indicated to be 
higher when this is the only valve, compared with an attic with two valves. This trend is observed both in 
both windy and calm weather. 
When there is a small wind velocity, the flowrates are in general higher when there is full infiltration, caus-
ing smaller differences than for 20 % infiltration. This could indicate that the flow out of underlay valves is

mainly driven by wind, when the weather is windy, whereas infiltration plays an important role when there

An imbalance is observed in the attic room with pressure equalization valve in the top, and for the attic

Figure 146 – Volume flow rates in and out of four attic rooms, according to tracer gas experiment with constant gas concentration 
in attic. 
As presented in figure 146, when a constant concentration is used only in the attic rooms, the derived 
flowrates through the valves are a bit higher in general, while being more than double for the attic with 
diffusion open underlay and two valves. This can expected since unintentional leakages can remove gas 
from the room, causing the flowrates to seem higher. These leakages could be to the inside the neighbor 
rooms, or through the roofing underlay. 
The observed trend was with the previous method that, the one valve in the underlay caused higher 
flowrates through this, than when having two valves. The opposite is observed now, which shows that the 
conditions can be very unstable and fluctuating from experiment to experiment. To clarify what causes 
these variations and when they occur, a longer experiment with continues measurement would be needed. 
8.4. Comparison of flowrates 
Some of the flowrate that have been derived by different methods are compared to find out how much 
they are alike. This will help clarify some of the strengths and weaknesses by the methods, and judge the 
usability for future experiments. 
The results compared from the CFD simulation are for the yearly average temperatures. Since correspond-
ing wind velocity in the CFD simulation is relatively low, a situation with calm wind is also used for the theo-
retical model, tracer gas experiment and pressure sensor measurements.

Figure 147 - Comparison flowrates from different methods. 
According to figure 147, the theoretical model is producing the highest flowrates in general. When there is 
two underlay valves, flow through these is lowest when measured with tracer gas, while the CFD simulation 
is in between. If there is only one valve, the flow through this is lowest when simulated with CFD.  
Some of the same trends are predicted by the different methods, even though there are some differences. 
A smaller infiltration leakage area was measured by all methods to cause a smaller infiltration flowrate. The 
infiltration flowrate is larger in all methods, when only one valve is used instead of two. The infiltration rate 
is in all cases lowest when simulated with CFD, actually being negative when simulated with two underlay 
valves. 
The flowrate through the underlay valve in top is measured to be lower, when one valve is used instead of 
two, by both the theoretical model and CFD simulation, but the opposite was measured in the tracer gas 
experiment. This could be because of unstable pressure conditions at the time of the tracer gas experi-
ment, which is not representative for a longer period.  
As presented, there are a number of differences between the flowrates achieved by the different methods. 
However since some of the same trends are predicted, it is expected that with a higher detail level of the 
CFD simulation and the theoretical model, and more precise measurements in the experimental setup, the 
predicted and measured flowrates would approach the actual flow. 
8.5. Limitations with method and future work 
This section explains some of the limitations of the methods used, and what can be done to improve these 
in the future, in order to fully find out how cold attic room design influences the conditions in them. 
In the experimental setup, measurements from pressure sensors were used to derive the infiltration 
flowrate. An advantage with this method is that the pressure conditions can be measured continuously, 
and time dependent relations thereby can be found. However, since the pressure differences were smaller 
than first expected, the calculated flowrates might not be completely accurate. Furthermore, the relation 
between the pressure and mass flow rate is only based on theoretical considerations and not measure-
ments. A validation of the infiltration flowrates by measuring them in another way is therefore needed.

141 
This could be done by installing a flow sensor in one of the infiltration tubes, or by making tracer gas exper-
iments designed to find the infiltration rate and compare this with the measured pressure.  
By tracer gas experiments, the air change was measured in the attics and container during a short period, 
at two different outdoor conditions. However, since only a short period was considered in these experi-
ments, the pressure conditions might be randomly affected by turbulence, and thereby generate conditions 
that are not representative for a longer period. In order to draw a conclusion that is based on statistically 
significant tendencies, more data is needed. Tracer gas experiments could be made during many different 
outdoor conditions in order to find general relations. In order to find the variation in time, longer experi-
ments could also be conducted during for example a whole day. 
In the CFD simulation, some simple boundary conditions were established. A pressure inlet was placed in 
the attic floor to simulate infiltration. Infiltration air from here would flow into the model in case of a nega-
tive pressure in the attic and out in case of overpressure. This boundary condition was defined this way, 
since only pressure conditions in the attics alone were considered in these simulations. However, this 
caused the infiltration flow to be out of the attic and into the dwelling in some cases, which is likely to be a 
result of a potential overpressure in the inside being disregarded. The gauge pressure, which describes the 
overpressure in the inside, could be defined from a calculated value, but would still have to be dependent 
on the variating pressure conditions in the setup. A sensitivity analysis should be made, in order to investi-
gate the influence of the pressure conditions inside. 
Another assumption regarding the boundary conditions in the CFD simulation, was the defined flowrate of 
air that was blown into the ventilation gap at the eaves and out at the top. However, the air pattern is not 
very likely always to be exactly like this, since side wind could cause air to be sucked out of the ventilation 
gap at both the eaves and the roof cowls. This would cause a negative pressure in the attic room, thus forc-
ing a corresponding amount of infiltration air into the attic room. Different variations in inlet velocity and 
direction should be investigated, in order to find out how this affects the flow pattern in cold attics and 
ultimately the moisture conditions in them. Furthermore, it is also necessary to investigate if the results are 
only valid for attic rooms, with a depth of one meter and a roof sloped 45°, or if the tendencies are more 
general.  An accurate model for airflows, where few assumptions would have to be made, is needed. This 
could be accomplished by simulating the whole construction, since the exact pressure conditions in the 
container under some given conditions are challenging to predict. The CFD model developed in this project 
can serve as a basis for more developed airflow models. 
The theoretical model on airflows was developed in order to find out if the theoretical basis supports the 
tendencies observed elsewhere. However, even though airflows that supports the conclusions were calcu-
lated, this model was not in very good balance in some zones. This shows that there is either something 
wrong with the solution method, the theoretical considerations, or the initial assumptions. A predefined 
and constant temperature have been assumed in all zones, air was considered to be an incompressible gas 
and moisture and heat storage in materials were neglected. The conclusions drawn from the model in this 
project should be validated with more advanced models. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:143==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
142 
8.6. Summary of discussion 
In the following, the research questions will be answered, showing how the parameter variations and 
boundary conditions for cold attic rooms affects the conditions and air movement in and around the attics. 
How does the design of a cold attic room affect mold growth promoting moisture conditions? 
Results from this investigation indicates that when using a diffusion open roofing underlay, no underlay 
valves should be used, since moisture sufficiently can be removed by diffusion through the roofing under-
lay. Establishing ventilation with two underlay valves indicated an increase in the risk of high moisture con-
tents and mold growth. Having only pressure equalization with one valve in the top of an attic room, makes 
the risk of moisture problems even higher, since negative pressures at the roof propagates to the attic 
room, making moist infiltration air enter by leakages in the vapor barrier. 
Attics with a diffusion tight roofing underlay had a higher risk of critical moisture conditions and mold 
growth. Having an underlay with two valves results in the least moisture problems, while having no under-
lay valves causes the greatest moisture problems. The improvement in the moisture conditions by having 
roofing underlay valves compared with no valves is less significant for a larger infiltration leakage. Heavy 
mold growth was observed by inspection and predicted theoretically, being most severe in attics with no 
valves. 
Relations were found between the influence of having underlay valves and the exterior conditions, for the 
attics with a diffusion open underlay. This relation showed to be especially strong when there is full infiltra-
tion. When comparing an attic without valves with an attic with one or two underlay valves, an increase in 
moisture content was most likely in case of a low moisture content in the exterior. Like the relation with 
moisture content, the highest increase in temperature was observed when the weather was cold. A higher 
increase in both temperature and moisture content can be expected for an attic room with only a pressure 
equalization valve in the top. 
How much air from the interior enters a cold attic rooms by infiltration? 
From measurements of pressure difference between attic and container, the expected infiltration flowrates 
were derived for the experimental setup. The average flowrate is into the attic, but flowrate the opposite 
way also occurs in short periods. A higher infiltration flowrate was indicated to be caused by having a larger 
infiltration area and/or underlay valves. The underlay valves are of most importance when the infiltration 
area is large. Considering the whole period measured, whether there is one or two underlay valves was not 
observed to have a big influence of infiltration flowrate. However, during the period of tracer gas meas-
urements, the infiltration was observed to be higher when only a pressure equalization valve in the top of 
the attic was placed instead of two valves.  
CFD simulations indicated that the infiltration rate is positive with one underlay valve, while being negative 
with two valves. Both the negative and positive infiltration flowrates becomes larger in cold weather com-
pared with an average situation. During the warm season, the infiltration flowrate was into the dwelling 
with both one and two underlay valves. This is likely to be a result of only considering pressure conditions 
in the attics and not the inside, but is a dangerous situation if a negative flowrate actually happens, since it 
could result in mycotoxin from mold to enter the dwelling. No significant infiltration flows were indicated 
from simulations with no valves in the underlay. 
==PAGE:144==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
143 
Measurements indicated a relation between wind velocity and direction with the infiltration flowrate. The 
influence was greatest for the attic rooms with underlay valves and especially for the attic rooms with a 
large infiltration area. In most cases, the wind tends to have most influence at wind speeds above 3 m/s.  
The infiltration flowrates relation with wind conditions was confirmed by a theoretical model, which indi-
cated that the infiltration is largest when the weather is windy. In general, this model also predicts the infil-
tration flowrate to be largest, when the infiltration area is large and only a pressure equalization valve is 
used. 
How much air enters and leaves the cold attic room by roofing underlay valves? 
The investigations indicated that outdoor air enters the attics by an underlay valve in the bottom, and 
leaves through a valve in the top of the attic room, if they are present.  
The tracer gas measurements and the theoretical model indicated that the top valve, in an attic room with 
two underlay valves, had a higher flowrate than the bottom valve, which makes sense since the infiltration 
flowrate here was into the attic. In the CFD simulation, where the infiltration flowrate was simulated to be 
negative for an attic with two valves, the flowrate was larger in through the bottom valve than out of the 
top valve. CFD simulation and the theoretical model indicated the flowrate through the top valve to be 
smaller when there is no underlay valve in the bottom. However, results from the tracer gas experiment 
are not completely consistent on this.  
During the cold season, the flowrate through the valves was indicated by CFD simulation to be higher. In 
the warm season the there is a small flow into the attic rooms through both valves. Windy weather was in 
most cases indicated by the different methods, to cause an increase in the flowrate through underlay 
valves. 
How does the air move inside a cold attic room and how is the temperature and moisture distributed? 
By CFD simulation, flows inside the attic rooms were simulated. At the average temperature and in the cold 
season where the incoming outdoor air is colder than the inside, buoyancy plays an important role. When 
having two underlay valves, the incoming cold air under the roof flows in to the attic at high velocity, where 
it distributes over the attic floor. Air circulates around the attic room, since this is heated up by the warmer 
inner surfaces and cooled down at the roofing underlay. In the ventilation gap, the air moves up along the 
underlay at a higher velocity than by the roof plates. 
When only one valve is used, some of the same trends are observed as for two valves. However, the miss-
ing air from the underlay valves in the bottom enables the infiltration air to enter the attic room, since 
there is no overpressure at the opening. The infiltrating air stands as a plume of heat and moisture in the 
middle of the room, which goes in direction of the top valve and out of this. 
When no valves were installed in the roofing underlay, air could not enter and leave the attic by these, but 
circulation was still possible because of temperature differences at the boundary conditions. 
In the warm season, with outdoor temperature being the same as indoor temperature, the air velocity in 
the ventilation gap was observed to be highest near the roof plates, which causes only a small flowrate into 
the attic room. The buoyancy driven flows inside the attics were also observed to be weakened, which hin-
ders the plume of warm infiltration air to be created. 
 
 
==PAGE:145==
Ventilation Design of Cold Attics for Avoiding Moisture-Related Problems 
 
144 
9. Conclusion 
The Danish Building Research Institute recommends that large cold attic rooms with a width of more than 
one meter and a diffusion open roofing underlay, should have ‘modest ventilation’ for example by having 
ventilation valves for every second truss. Similar recommendations are presented by BYG-ERFA, which 
states that ventilation should be established by installing valves in the underlay, alternating in top and bot-
tom in attic rooms with a diffusion open roofing underlay, and that attics with a diffusion tight underlay 
should always be ventilated. 
An experimental setup have been established at the Technical University of Denmark to test these recom-
mendations. This setup have been designed to test parameter variations in north turned cold attic rooms, 
with a width of one meter, a 45° inclined roof and a leakage in the vapor barrier within the allowed accord-
ing to Danish Building Regulations.  
From analysis of measurement data and mold sampling in the setup, it can be recommended not to install 
underlay valves in cold attic rooms with a diffusion open roofing underlay, since this is was indicated to 
cause an increase in the risk of having a high humidity and mold growth in the attic rooms. In particular, 
pressure equalization by a valve opening in top of the room, resulted in the highest risk of mold growth and 
should therefore be avoided. The recommendation of ventilating attics with a diffusion tight underlay can 
be confirmed, even though this type of roofing underlay cannot be recommended because of an increased 
risk of moisture accumulation in the cold attic rooms. 
By use of Computational Fluid Dynamics (CFD) simulations and theoretical considerations, the influence of 
underlay valves on flow conditions in cold attics was also investigated. It was found that outdoor air enters 
the attics by openings in the bottom and leaves by openings in the top, if they are present. The flowrate of 
infiltrating air entering the cold attic rooms was indicated to be larger, when only one valve was installed in 
the top of the attic room. A positive relation was also found between wind velocity and flowrate of infiltra-
tion and airflow through underlay valves.  
The pressure distribution in ventilated parts of a construction, generated by wind and buoyancy forces are 
difficult to predict, but still crucial to indoor climate and moisture conditions in the building. Further 
knowledge on how the pressure distribution is in different situations and for different designs, is therefore 
needed. This knowledge could be achieved by making simulation of whole constructions in CFD, combined 
with accurate flow, air change and pressure measurements in different ventilated zones.

cementbaseret produkt, der specielt 
er udviklet til at mod­stå de strengeste 
klimatiske­ på­virk­ninger. Pladen er opbygget­ 
af mange tynde, sammen­pressede lag, der 
hver især kan betragtes som en armeret 
plade. Denne opbygning i kombination 
med bølgeformen giver pladen styrke og 
stivhed.

en ubehandlet over­flade. Pladen fremtræ-

cement­baserede materialer, frem­stillet af 
grå cement. Kalkudfældninger og farvenu-
ancer kan forekomme på bølgeplader og 
er uden betydning for holdbarheden og 
betragtes ikke som berittiget reklamati-
onsgrundlag.

IcoAir ventilationsstuds 
Anvendelse 
IcoAir ventilationsstuds anvendes til ventilation af undertage. IcoAir kan monteres såvel 
udefra som indefra. 
 
Produktdata 
                         Blad nr. 166
Gyldigt fra 
27.02.2013 
Dimension, mm 
 
a 
100 
 
b 
153 
    c   
36 
 
 
Farve 
Sort 
Opbygning 
Vulkaniseret termoplastisk gummi 
Montage 
Studsen monteres ved tagryg mellem 2. og 3. øverste 
lægte. IcoAir placeres på undertaget 15 cm fra spær og 
midt mellem lægter. Der udskæres et hul ved hjælp af den 
medfølgende skæreskabelon.  
 
Montage udefra: 
IcoAir åbnes og og føres ned i hullet, så undertaget 
kommer til at være mellem dækslet og underdelen. Når 
undertaget er på plads i studsen, vrides dækslet tilbage 
på plads og studsen drejes rundt, så undertaget kommer 
på plads. 
Montage indefra: 
IcoAir presses sammen, så undertaget er placeret mellem 
dækslet og underdelen. Når undertaget er på plads i 
studsen, drejes studsen rundt, så undertaget kommer på 
plads mellem dækslet og underdelen. 
Montage på fast undertag: 
Der bores hul i underlaget med Ø105 mm kopbor.   
Icopal Tagklæber påføres rundt om hullet. IcoAir trykkes 
på plads, således at klæbemassen mases ud under 
ventilationsstudsen. 
 
Tekniske data 
 
Arealer 
Rørlysning, cm2 
 
50 
Egenskaber 
IcoAir ventilationsstuds sikrer effektiv ventilation i tagkonstruktionen. Produktet er forsynet 
med insekt- og bladfang i dækslet.

Ydervægsrist
YGC
Beskrivelse
YGC er en cirkulær jalousirist for montage i ydervægge, som
kan anvendes til både friskluftsindtag samt afkast. YGC er
som standard i størrelse Ø100-500 forsynet med net. Ø630-
1250 kan mod bestilling ligeledes leveres med net. Nettets
maskestørrelse er 10 x 10 mm.
Vedligeholdelse
Risten demonteres for at opnå fri adgang til kanal. De
udvendige dele aftørres med en fugtig klud.
Dimensioner
* er forsynet med 2 stk. Ø 4,2 mm skruehuller i siden for
montering.
** er forsynet med 3 stk. Ø 4,0 mm skruehuller i fronten for
montering.
Ød > 315 mm , ingen forborede huller til montering.
Af (m2) = Fri areal.

Appendix h
==PAGE:162==
The Gill WindSonic is a low-cost anemometer, which utilises Gill’s proven 
ultrasonic technology to provide wind speed and direction data via one 
serial or two analogue outputs.  To confirm correct operation, outputs are 
transmitted together with an instrument status code.  
With a robust, corrosion-free polycarbonate housing, this small, lightweight 
wind sensor is recommended for use in harsh environmental conditions and 
is particularly suited to marine & offshore (ships, data buoys) and land based 
installations.  The WindSonic has no moving parts, offering maintenance-free 
operation in a wide range of applications.

When it comes to measuring humidity with the highest accuracy, the HygroClip2 series of probes is in a 
class of its own.  Based on AirChip3000 technology, its unique features are sure to impress.
In combination with advances in sensor technology and integration, the HygroClip2 will provide superb 
precision and state-of-the-art functionality, taking humidity measurement to a whole new level of 
performance and reliability (< 0.8 %rh / 0.1 K).
Specify the best:
HygroClip2 advantages at a glance.
AirChip3000
• Compensates humidity and temperature
	
over 30,000 reference points
• 2,000 data point memory
• Dew/frost point calculation
• Sensor auto-diagnostics and automatic correction
• Active alarming and information
• ASIC (Application Specific Integrated Circuit),
	
micro-controller and EEPROM on one chip
Innovative filter technology
• The latest in filter technology provides a high level of
	 protection and maintains the optimum measurement
	 environment for the Hygromer humidity and Pt100
	 temperature sensors
Maximum flexibility
• The UART 1 interface and user scalable analog outputs allow 
the probe to be used as a stand-alone device that can also 
be effortlessly integrated into an OEM application
• The probes can be interchanged without adjustment
	
1 Universal Asynchronous Receiver Transmitter
Fast response
• By decoupling the Pt100 temperature sensor from
	 the probe, thermal response is significantly improved

Probes for Industrial Applications
With a variety of mechanical designs, our industrial probes 
are suitable for use in the most demanding environments. 
Operating range 0…100  %rh, -100…200  °C (-148…392  °F) and 
0…400 bar (0…5,800 PSI).
Probes for Handheld Instruments
Almost any conceivable measurement task can be performed 
with our range of hand-held probes. Depending on probe type, 
the operating range is between 0…100 %rh and -100…200 °C.
Probe options.
Whether you need a simple climate probe for measuring ambient conditions, or a more sophisticated cable probe for high temperature 
and other special applications, we can provide you with the ideal HygroClip2 probe to suit your requirements.
All HygroClip2 probes can be individually calibrated and adjusted to maximize measurement precision where you need it the most. 
This capability is exactly what makes every probe in the product range a high-end solution for your application.
Applications.
In addition to individual probes, the HygroClip2 product family includes handheld instruments, transmitters, data loggers and 
custom designed products, all based on cutting-edge AirChip3000 digital technology. Maintenance in the field is easy; the HygroClip2 
probes can be interchanged within a matter of seconds without the need for system recalibration. We will gladly advise you which 
specific model to use to achieve the best possible results for your application.
==PAGE:167==
85 / 110 mm
Ø 15 mm
SW 14
Factory Adjustment.
Three different adjustment profiles are available from the factory; this 
means that measurement accuracy can be matched to the application 
need. The custom adjustment profile consists of 20 Humidity values 
at 3 Temperatures to ensure optimum performance over a required 
working range.  Calibration data is stored within the probe and can be 
retrieved later for audit purposes.
Probe Output Signal.
When connected to a PC the HygroClip2 probes can be rescaled with 
different ranges to suit the application need. It is also possible to 
assign the internally calculated dew or frost point value to one of these 
outputs; thus converting the HygroClip2 into a dew point probe.
Sensor Diagnostics.
The intelligence of AirChip3000 technology enables advanced sensor 
diagnostics. Should the RH sensor deviate from factory defined 
parameters (for example because of chemical contamination) 
measurement values can be automatically compensated and a digital 
alarm triggered. The HygroClip2 probe can also be programmed to 
generate an alarm in the form of pre-defined analog output signals 
in the event of a problem with either the RH or temperature sensor. 
The user has full control over these features using ROTRONIC HW4 
software.
Data Logging and Alarm Generation.
Up to 2,000 measurement values can be stored in the HygroClip2 
probe; the user is able to configure the measurement interval, set 
alarm limits, scale the output signal and download data using optional 
ROTRONIC HW4 software. The HygroClip2 probe can be programmed 
with set limits to generate an alarm which is available when the probe 
is communicating with a PC or compatible ROTRONIC device. Thus, 
the HygroClip2 can be integrated in any application.

www.sensirion.com  
Sensirion -  the sensor company 
SDP1000 / SDP2000
Low Range Differential Pressure Sensor  
for Air and Non-Aggressive Gases 
Data Sheet – v5
 
− For cost sensitive HVAC and medical OEM applications 
− Unsurpassed performance thanks to CMOSens® technology 
− Offset and hysteresis free 
− Excellent accuracy and reproducibility even below 10 Pa 
− Fully calibrated and temperature compensated 
− Linear or square root extracted output characteristics available 
− Not sensitive to the mounting orientation 
− Direct PCB mounting with simple snap-on system 
 
 
SDP1000 / SDP2000 Product Summary
The SDP1000 / SDP2000 differential pressure sensors 
for air cover the following measurement ranges:  –5 to 
125 Pa (0.5 inch H2O), -20 to 500 Pa (2 inch H2O), and -
100 to 3500 Pa (14 inch H2O).  
Mounted in a rugged, chemically inert PPS housing the 
SDP1000 / SDP2000 differential pressure sensors 
feature a unique dynamic range, zero offset and 
unsurpassed long term stability. This makes it an ideal fit 
for demanding yet cost sensitive OEM applications  in 
HVAC and medical equipment. 
The devices are supplied with 5.0 V and provide a 
0.25…4.0 V output. Although the output of the 
SDP1000 / SDP2000 differential pressure sensor is 
analog, the internal linearization and temperature 
compensation is performed digitally. This results in a 
superior accuracy, outstanding resolution (up to 0.05 
Pa), and lowest temperature dependence. The SDP1000 
is available with a linear or square root extracted output 
characteristics at the same price. Since fully 
exchangeable just a few external electronic components 
around the SDP1000 / SDP2000 make a high quality 
differential sensor transmitter with e.g. an 0...10 V output 
or other interfaces (see Figure 8). 
Its leading performance is based on Sensirion’s 
proprietary CMOSens® sensor technology which 
combines the sensor element with amplification and A/D 
conversion on one single silicon chip. The differential 
pressure is measured by a thermal sensor element. In 
contrast to other thermal differential pressure sensors 
only a very small amount of air is required (see 
Figure 6). This leads to a reliable operation even under 
harsh conditions. In comparison to membrane based 
sensors the SDP1000 / SDP2000 differential pressure 
sensors show an extended measurement range, better 
offset stability and improved reproducibility even at 
lowest pressure ranges (see Figure 1 and 2). In addition 
the SDP1000 / SDP2000 is  robust against pressure 
bursts and shows no sensitivity to the mounting 
orientation. 
SDP1000 / SDP2000 is fully RoHS compliant. 
 
Applications  
 
 
- Medical 
- HVAC 
- Automotive 
- Process Automation

1.1 Temperature Compensation 
The SDP1000 / SDP2000 differential pressure 
sensors feature a sophisticated built-in temperature 
compensation circuit. The temperature is measured 
on the CMOSens chip by means of a PTAT bandgap 
reference temperature sensor.  Its data is fed into a 
compensation circuit which is also integrated on the 
CMOSens® sensor chip. No external temperature 
compensation is therefore required. 
1.2 Altitude Correction 
The SDP1000 / SDP2000 differential pressure 
sensors achieve their unsurpassed performance by 
using a dynamic measurement principle, i.e. an 
applied differential pressure forces a small air flow 
through the sensor. This results in a dependence of 
the indicated differential pressure on the ambient air 
density. While the temperature effect is compensated 
(see Paragraph 1.1) the altitude above sea level has 
an influence on the SDP1000 / SDP2000 output. If 
desired, this effect can be compensated by a 
correction factor according to the following equation: 
 
Dpeff = Dpsensor * Pcal / Pamb 
 
where Dpeff is the effective differential pressure, 
Dpsensor the differential pressure indicated by the 
SDP1000 / SDP2000, Pcal the absolute pressure 
during calibration (966 mbar) and Pamb the actual 
ambient absolute pressure. 
 
This leads to the following correction factors:  
 
Table 3: Altitude correction factors. 
 
Altitude
[meter] 
Ambient 
Pressure 
(Pamb) [mbar] 
Correction Factor 
Pcal  / Pamb

Example: 
 
The SDP1000 is used at 750 m above sea level. The 
output of the SDP1000 shows 0.5 V, which 
corresponds to Dpsensor = 33.3 Pa. Taking into account 
the correction factor Pcal / Pamb = 1.04 the effective 
differential pressure Dpeff is 33.3 Pa * 1.04 = 34.6 Pa. 
 
Note: 
 
In many HVAC applications such as filter monitoring, 
fan/ventilator control or air flow measurement the 
described effect is actually welcome since at the end 
the mass flow and not volume flow is the effective 
value to control.

Electrical Specifications 
2.1 Power Supply 
The SDP1000 / SDP2000 differential pressure 
sensors require a stable voltage supply of 5 V. 
Influence of the supply voltage variation on the offset 
and the sensitivity are given in Table 5. 
2.2 Voltage Output 
The SDP1000 / SDP2000 features a voltage output 
from 0.25 V to 4.0 V (Figure 2, 3). An output voltage 
below 0.25 V indicates a negative differential 
pressure. This range is not calibrated however. 
Exempt from this statement is the SDP1000-L025, 
which features a fully bidirectional calibration with 0.2 
to 4.0 V output. 
The resistive load at the output pin should be larger 
than 20 kOhm. The capacitive load at the output pin 
must not be larger than 200 pF. If the design shows a 
larger capacity at the output pin an additional resistor 
is required in series at the output (e.g. 620 Ohm).

Physical Dimensions and Mounting Information
3.1 Housing 
The SDP1000 / SDP2000 differential pressure sensor 
is mounted in chemically inert PPS housing. The 
rugged package has been designed to withstand 
continuous overpressures of at least 1 bar (14.5 PSI). 
Burst pressure is > 2 bar (29 PSI) 
 
 
 
The physical dimensions and mounting information is 
given in Figure 4 and 5. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 4: Pin out and physical dimensions in mm (inch). The 
drawing is not to scale. 
Figure 5: SDP1000 / SDP2000 PCB footprint. The 
drawing is not to scale. 
 
3.2 Soldering Instructions 
The SDP1000 / SDP2000 differential pressure sensor 
can be wave soldered. Direct reflow soldering is not 
recommended since it may affect the accuracy. 
If reflow soldering is required Sensirion recommends 
to use an SMD connector (e.g. type Samtec SSM- 
 
 
 
103-L-SV) and to mount the SDP1000 / SDP2000 
after soldering.

3.3 Connecting Hose 
Sensirion recommends a hose with an inner diameter 
of 1/8 to 3/20 inch (3.18 to 3.8mm). Due to the 
dynamic measurement principle, a small air flow is 
required (Figure 6) which 
 
 
 
leads to a dependence on the length of the hose 
(Figure 7). Tubes up to 1 m show less than 1 % error 
of the measured value (Figure 7). 
 
 
Figure 6: Typical air flow through the SDP1000 / SDP2000. 
Please note: 1 sccm = 1 cm3/min at 0°C and  
1013 mbar pressure (1 sccm = 0.001 norm liter). 
Figure 7: Influence of the length of the connecting hose on 
the accuracy (using 3/16 inch inner diameter). 
Example: a 50 Pa difference pressure is shown as 
49.8 Pa when using 0.5 m tube with 3/16 inch 
diameter.

The inherent design of this component causes it to be 
sensitive to electrostatic discharge (ESD). To prevent 
ESD-induced damage and/or degradation, take 
customary and statutory ESD precautions when 
handling this product.

information.
